"""Top-k via register-based insertion scan + shared-memory warp merge + block bitonic sort.

Design:
  - Per-thread: sorted-descending buffer of k (value, index) pairs in registers.
    Insertion uses binary search + shift.  Only 2k registers needed per thread.
  - Warp merge: warps take turns using shared memory.  Each warp copies its
    per-thread buffers to shared memory, does a butterfly merge (reads partner
    data from the stable shared-memory copy, insertion-sorts each element
    into its register buffer), writes back for the next mask round.
    8 barriers between warps (~1 µs total).
  - Block merge: lane-0 of each warp writes its result to the block merge
    buffer; a bitonic sort extracts the block-wide top-k.
"""

from __future__ import annotations

import os
import tempfile

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load

OP_TYPE = "topk"
SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]


CUDA_SOURCE = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <c10/cuda/CUDAStream.h>

#define MAX_K 64
#define WARP_SIZE 32
#define BLOCK_SIZE 256
#define NUM_WARPS (BLOCK_SIZE / WARP_SIZE)
#define MERGE_BUF_SIZE 512
#define NEG_INF __int_as_float(0xff7fffff)

// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
__global__ void __launch_bounds__(BLOCK_SIZE, 1)
topk_scan_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    float* __restrict__ out_values,
    long* __restrict__ out_indices,
    float* __restrict__ inter_values,
    int* __restrict__   inter_indices,
    int batch_size, int n, int k, int blocks_per_row)
{
    int row       = blockIdx.x / blocks_per_row;
    int block_col = blockIdx.x % blocks_per_row;
    if (row >= batch_size) return;

    int tid     = threadIdx.x;
    int lane    = tid & 31;
    int warp_id = tid >> 5;

    // Shared memory: warp_sm (WARP_SIZE*MAX_K elements, 16 KB) + block merge (4 KB)
    __shared__ float warp_sm_val[WARP_SIZE * MAX_K];
    __shared__ int   warp_sm_idx[WARP_SIZE * MAX_K];
    __shared__ float block_val[MERGE_BUF_SIZE];
    __shared__ int   block_idx[MERGE_BUF_SIZE];

    #define WSM_V(l) (warp_sm_val + (l) * MAX_K)
    #define WSM_I(l) (warp_sm_idx + (l) * MAX_K)

    // ---- per-thread sorted buffer (registers) ----
    float my_val[MAX_K];
    int   my_idx[MAX_K];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) { my_val[i] = NEG_INF; my_idx[i] = -1; }

    int chunk_start = block_col * (n / blocks_per_row);
    int chunk_end   = (block_col == blocks_per_row - 1) ? n
                     : (block_col + 1) * (n / blocks_per_row);

    // ---- Phase 1: per-thread scan (insertion sort, all threads concurrent) ----
    for (int idx = chunk_start + tid; idx < chunk_end; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) {
        float val = __ldg(input + row * n + idx);
        if (val <= my_val[k - 1]) continue;

        int lo = 0, hi = k - 1;
        #pragma unroll 1
        while (lo < hi) {
            int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1;
            if (my_val[mid] < val) hi = mid;
            else                   lo = mid + 1;
        }
        int pos = lo;
        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = k - 1; i > pos; i--) {
            my_val[i] = my_val[i - 1];
            my_idx[i] = my_idx[i - 1];
        }
        my_val[pos] = val;
        my_idx[pos] = idx;
    }

    // ---- Phase 2: warp merge (sequential warps, shared memory) ----
    for (int w = 0; w < NUM_WARPS; w++) {
        if (warp_id == w) {
            // 2a. Copy my buffer → shared memory (pad with NEG_INF)
            for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) {
                int off = lane * MAX_K + i;
                if (i < k) {
                    WSM_V(lane)[i] = my_val[i];
                    WSM_I(lane)[i] = my_idx[i];
                } else {
                    WSM_V(lane)[i] = NEG_INF;
                    WSM_I(lane)[i] = -1;
                }
            }

            // 2b. Butterfly merge reading partner data from stable shared memory
            for (int mask = 1; mask < WARP_SIZE; mask <<= 1) {
                int partner = lane ^ mask;
                #pragma unroll 1
                for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) {
                    float p_val = WSM_V(partner)[i];
                    int   p_idx = WSM_I(partner)[i];

                    if (p_val <= my_val[k - 1]) continue;

                    int lo = 0, hi = k - 1;
                    #pragma unroll 1
                    while (lo < hi) {
                        int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1;
                        if (my_val[mid] < p_val) hi = mid;
                        else                     lo = mid + 1;
                    }
                    int pos = lo;
                    #pragma unroll
                    for (int j = k - 1; j > pos; j--) {
                        my_val[j] = my_val[j - 1];
                        my_idx[j] = my_idx[j - 1];
                    }
                    my_val[pos] = p_val;
                    my_idx[pos] = p_idx;
                }

                // Write merged result back to shared memory for next mask round
                for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) {
                    WSM_V(lane)[i] = my_val[i];
                    WSM_I(lane)[i] = my_idx[i];
                }
            }

            // 2c. Lane 0 writes warp result to block merge buffer
            int base = w * k;
            for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) {
                block_val[base + i] = WSM_V(0)[i];
                block_idx[base + i] = WSM_I(0)[i];
            }
        }
        __syncthreads();  // hand off shared memory to next warp
    }

    // Init unwritten portion of block merge buffer
    for (int i = tid + NUM_WARPS * k; i < MERGE_BUF_SIZE; i += BLOCK_SIZE) {
        block_val[i] = NEG_INF;
        block_idx[i] = -1;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // ---- Phase 3: block merge (bitonic sort descending) ----
    #pragma unroll 1
    for (int stage = 2; stage <= MERGE_BUF_SIZE; stage <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll 1
        for (int step = stage >> 1; step > 0; step >>= 1) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int e = 0; e < MERGE_BUF_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE; e++) {
                int i = tid * (MERGE_BUF_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE) + e;
                int partner = i ^ step;
                if (i < partner && i < MERGE_BUF_SIZE) {
                    bool ascending = ((i & stage) != 0);
                    float av = block_val[i]; int ai = block_idx[i];
                    float bv = block_val[partner]; int bi = block_idx[partner];
                    bool swap = ascending ? (av > bv) : (av < bv);
                    if (swap) {
                        block_val[i] = bv; block_idx[i] = bi;
                        block_val[partner] = av; block_idx[partner] = ai;
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }

    // ---- write output ----
    if (blocks_per_row == 1) {
        if (tid < k) {
            out_values[row * k + tid]  = block_val[tid];
            out_indices[row * k + tid] = (long)block_idx[tid];
        }
    } else {
        if (tid < k) {
            int off = row * blocks_per_row * k + block_col * k + tid;
            inter_values[off] = block_val[tid];
            inter_indices[off] = block_idx[tid];
        }
    }
}

// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
__global__ void topk_merge_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ inter_values,
    const int*   __restrict__ inter_indices,
    float* __restrict__ out_values,
    long* __restrict__ out_indices,
    int batch_size, int blocks_per_row, int k)
{
    int row = blockIdx.x;
    if (row >= batch_size) return;

    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int total_elems = blocks_per_row * k;

    __shared__ float merge_val[MERGE_BUF_SIZE];
    __shared__ int   merge_idx[MERGE_BUF_SIZE];

    for (int i = tid; i < MERGE_BUF_SIZE; i += BLOCK_SIZE) {
        merge_val[i] = NEG_INF;
        merge_idx[i] = -1;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    int row_off = row * blocks_per_row * k;
    for (int i = tid; i < total_elems; i += BLOCK_SIZE) {
        merge_val[i] = inter_values[row_off + i];
        merge_idx[i] = inter_indices[row_off + i];
    }
    __syncthreads();

    #pragma unroll 1
    for (int stage = 2; stage <= MERGE_BUF_SIZE; stage <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll 1
        for (int step = stage >> 1; step > 0; step >>= 1) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int e = 0; e < MERGE_BUF_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE; e++) {
                int i = tid * (MERGE_BUF_SIZE / BLOCK_SIZE) + e;
                int partner = i ^ step;
                if (i < partner && i < MERGE_BUF_SIZE) {
                    bool ascending = ((i & stage) != 0);
                    float av = merge_val[i]; int ai = merge_idx[i];
                    float bv = merge_val[partner]; int bi = merge_idx[partner];
                    bool swap = ascending ? (av > bv) : (av < bv);
                    if (swap) {
                        merge_val[i] = bv; merge_idx[i] = bi;
                        merge_val[partner] = av; merge_idx[partner] = ai;
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }

    if (tid < k) {
        out_values[row * k + tid]  = merge_val[tid];
        out_indices[row * k + tid] = (long)merge_idx[tid];
    }
}

// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
torch::Tensor launch_topk_scan(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor out_values,
    torch::Tensor out_indices,
    torch::Tensor inter_values,
    torch::Tensor inter_indices,
    int64_t batch_size, int64_t n, int64_t k, int64_t blocks_per_row)
{
    auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(input.device().index());
    int grid = (int)batch_size * (int)blocks_per_row;
    dim3 block(BLOCK_SIZE);
    topk_scan_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, stream>>>(
        input.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_values.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_indices.data_ptr<long>(),
        inter_values.data_ptr<float>(),
        inter_indices.data_ptr<int>(),
        (int)batch_size, (int)n, (int)k, (int)blocks_per_row);
    return out_values;
}

torch::Tensor launch_topk_merge(
    torch::Tensor inter_values,
    torch::Tensor inter_indices,
    torch::Tensor out_values,
    torch::Tensor out_indices,
    int64_t batch_size, int64_t blocks_per_row, int64_t k)
{
    auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(inter_values.device().index());
    dim3 block(BLOCK_SIZE);
    topk_merge_kernel<<<(int)batch_size, block, 0, stream>>>(
        inter_values.data_ptr<float>(),
        inter_indices.data_ptr<int>(),
        out_values.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_indices.data_ptr<long>(),
        (int)batch_size, (int)blocks_per_row, (int)k);
    return out_values;
}
"""

CPP_SOURCE = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>

torch::Tensor launch_topk_scan(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor out_values,
    torch::Tensor out_indices,
    torch::Tensor inter_values,
    torch::Tensor inter_indices,
    int64_t batch_size, int64_t n, int64_t k, int64_t blocks_per_row);

torch::Tensor launch_topk_merge(
    torch::Tensor inter_values,
    torch::Tensor inter_indices,
    torch::Tensor out_values,
    torch::Tensor out_indices,
    int64_t batch_size, int64_t blocks_per_row, int64_t k);

PYBIND11_MODULE(TORCH_EXTENSION_NAME, m) {
    m.def("launch_topk_scan", &launch_topk_scan, "launch_topk_scan");
    m.def("launch_topk_merge", &launch_topk_merge, "launch_topk_merge");
}
"""

# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
_topk_module = None


def _get_module():
    global _topk_module
    if _topk_module is not None:
        return _topk_module
    build_dir = tempfile.mkdtemp(prefix="topk_build_")
    cuda_path = os.path.join(build_dir, "kernels.cu")
    cpp_path = os.path.join(build_dir, "bindings.cpp")
    with open(cuda_path, "w") as f:
        f.write(CUDA_SOURCE)
    with open(cpp_path, "w") as f:
        f.write(CPP_SOURCE)
    _topk_module = load(
        name="topk_bitonic_ops",
        sources=[cpp_path, cuda_path],
        extra_cflags=["-fpermissive", "-Wno-error"],
        extra_cuda_cflags=["-arch=sm_86", "--use_fast_math"],
        verbose=False,
    )
    return _topk_module


def _topk_cuda(x: torch.Tensor, k: int) -> tuple[torch.Tensor, torch.Tensor]:
    mod = _get_module()
    batch, n = x.shape
    device = x.device
    out_values = torch.empty(batch, k, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)
    out_indices = torch.empty(batch, k, dtype=torch.int64, device=device)
    mod.launch_topk_scan(
        x, out_values, out_indices,
        torch.empty(1, device=device),
        torch.empty(1, dtype=torch.int32, device=device),
        batch, n, k, 1,
    )
    return out_values, out_indices


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
batch = 64
n = 8192
k = 8


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
        return _topk_cuda(x, self.k)


def get_inputs():
    x = torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32)
    return [x]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [batch, n, k]
The kernel passes correctness on all 5 shapes and all seeds/numeric-stress cases. **Performance results vs `torch.topk` (eager):** | Shape | Solution | Eager | Ratio | |-------|----------|-------|-------| | (1, 131072, 64) | 6.33 ms | 0.071 ms | 89x | | (64, 8192, 8) | 0.30 ms | 0.098 ms | 3.1x | | (32, 16384, 32) | 1.40 ms | 0.075 ms | 18.7x | | (16, 12000, 16) | 0.56 ms | 0.070 ms | 8.0x | | (128, 4096, 1) | 0.14 ms | 0.044 ms | 3.2x | Peak fraction: 0.0019 (LOW — failed to reach 10% of DRAM bandwidth). **Root cause:** The register-based per-thread insertion sort + per-element warp merge with O(k²) insertion overhead for k=64 is fundamentally slower than `torch.topk`'s CUB-backed radix-select kernel (O(n) with low constants). At these small problem sizes where launch overhead is significant, torch.topk's fused multi-phase approach dominates. The kernel IS correct — it just can't compete with NVIDIA's heavily-tuned radix-select on this hardware. To beat torch.topk on this shape mix would require either a full radix-select implementation or leveraging CUB's `DeviceRadixSort` with a selection predicate — both significant engineering efforts beyond a single-session kernel rewrite.

Approaches: 1. **Radix select** (bucket-based): Use radix sort to find the top-k values. This is O(n) per pass. 2. **Bitonic top-k**: For power-of-2 n and k that are powers of 2, a bitonic network can be very efficient. But n=12000 is not a power of 2. 3. **Warp/block-level selection**: Each block processes a chunk, finds local top-k using register-based heaps or bitonic sort, then merge across blocks. 4. **CUB's DeviceRadixSort + selection**: Use CUB's radix sort to sort, then take top-k. Each block loads elements into shared memory, does a bitonic sort to find local top-k 3. Write local top-k to global memory 4. Merge phase: another kernel or a second pass merges the partial results But actually, for most of these shapes (n <= 16384), a single block can handle the entire row. Only n=131072 needs cross-block coordination. Let me think about this more carefully. **Shape analysis:** - (1, 131072, 64): 1 row of 131072 elements, need top 64. One block = 1024 threads, each thread loads ~128 elements. Actually 131072/1024 = 128 elements per thread. That's manageable in registers. - (64, 8192, 8): 64 rows of 8192 each, need top 8. Each thread loads `n / blockDim.x` elements into registers 2. Threads cooperate to build a bitonic sequence, then use a bitonic merge network to extract top-k 3. Write results to global memory But the challenge with using a full bitonic sort for top-k is that we need to handle non-power-of-2 sizes (12000) and we don't need a full sort — we just need the top k. Actually, let me reconsider. The most robust approach for top-k with arbitrary n and k: **Three-phase approach:** 1. **Local top-k**: Each thread block processes a chunk, finds local top-k using a heap or bitonic sort in registers/shared memory 2. **Global merge**: If multiple blocks per row, merge partial results 3. **Output**: Write final top-k sorted descending For the register-level bitonic sort, I can use the "bitonic top-k" algorithm where we build a bitonic sequence and then do a bitonic selection to get the top k. Let me look at what approach other high-performance top-k implementations use: CUB's `DeviceRadixSort` + selection is one option, but it's not a single fused kernel. Actually, for the most efficient implementation, let me consider: **Radix-select approach (most promising for large n, small k):** - Use a most-significant-bit (MSB) radix sort approach - In each pass, count how many elements have the current bit set - If count >= k, keep only those elements; otherwise keep all and adjust k - This is O(n * bits) but bits = 32, and each pass is very fast But actually, for float32 values, we need to handle the sign bit and exponent properly. The radix select approach for floats requires bit-level manipulation. Let me think about what's simplest and most performant... **Actually, let me just go with a well-optimized block-level bitonic top-k approach:** The key idea: 1. Each block handles one row (or part of a row for n=131072) 2. Load elements into registers 3. Use a bitonic sorting network within the warp/block to find top-k 4. Write results For n <= 16384, one block per row works well. For n = 131072, we need ~4 blocks per row (131072 / 32768 = 4 blocks, each with 1024 threads, 32 elements per thread). Wait, let me reconsider the thread count. Each SM can handle multiple blocks. With 1024 threads per block, each thread processes `ceil(n / 1024)` elements for its row. For n=131072: 131072 / 1024 = 128 elements per thread. Each element is 4 bytes (fp32) + we need indices (int64 = 8 bytes). So 128 * (4 + 8) = 1536 bytes minimum, plus sorting overhead. This is a lot of register pressure. Alternative: Use shared memory. Load elements into shared memory, then do the sort there. Let me think about the shared memory approach: **Shared memory bitonic sort:** - Load chunk into shared memory (pairs of (value, index)) - Do bitonic sort in shared memory - Take top k But shared memory per block is limited: 48KB on RTX 3090 (configurable up to 100KB but that reduces L1 cache). For n=131072 with 1024 threads, if we process in chunks of ~4096 elements at a time (4096 * 8 bytes for fp32+int32 = 32KB), we need 32 passes. Hmm, that's too many passes. Let me think of a better approach. **Better approach: Register-based partial sort with shared memory merge** 1. Each thread loads `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` elements into registers (value + index pairs) 2. Each thread does a bitonic sort of its own elements in registers 3. Warp-level merge: within each warp, threads cooperate to merge their sorted lists 4. Block-level merge: warps cooperate to merge their results 5. The block now has the top k from its chunk 6. If multiple blocks per row, another merge pass is needed This is essentially the approach used in many high-performance top-k implementations. Let me design this more concretely. For a thread block of 256 threads (good occupancy on 3090): - Each thread processes `n / 256` elements - For n=131072: 512 elements per thread. That's 512 * 8 bytes = 4KB of registers per thread - too much. With 1024 threads: - n=131072: 128 elements per thread. 128 * 8 = 1KB registers. Still high but manageable if we use fewer items. Alternative: process in waves. Each wave loads W elements per thread, processes them, keeps top-k, then merges with next wave. Let me design a wave-based approach: **Wave-based top-k:** 1. Each thread processes `WAVE_SIZE` elements at a time 2. In registers, maintain a sorted list of top-k candidates 3. For each wave, load new elements, merge with existing top-k 4. After all waves, the block has the top-k for its chunk 5. Merge across blocks if needed The insertion/merge into a sorted list of k elements: we can use a bitonic merge network for efficiency. For k <= 64, maintaining a sorted list of k elements in registers is feasible (64 * 8 = 512 bytes for k=64). Let me design this with: - 256 threads per block (good occupancy) - Each thread maintains top-k in registers (or we can distribute across threads in a warp) Actually, let me look at this from a different angle. The most efficient GPU top-k implementations I've seen use: 1. **Radix select** (for arbitrary k/n ratios): Process bits from MSB to LSB, partitioning elements. This is what CUB uses internally. 2. **Block-level selection with shared memory**: Load into shared memory, do repeated partitioning (like quickselect). 3. **Bitonic top-k in registers**: For small k, this can be very efficient. For the specific shapes here, let me think about what approach gives the best performance: - (1, 131072, 64): k=64, n=131072. k/n = 0.05%. Radix select would be great here. Or a block-level approach. - (64, 8192, 8): k=8, n=8192. k/n = 0.1%. Block-level with shared memory. - (32, 16384, 32): k=32, n=16384. k/n = 0.2%. - (16, 12000, 16): k=16, n=12000. k/n = 0.13%. - (128, 4096, 1): k=1, n=4096. Simple reduction. For all shapes, k is very small compared to n. This suggests a selection-based approach rather than a full sort. **My plan:** I'll implement a CUDA kernel using a **radix-select** approach, which is the most efficient for small k relative to n. The idea: For float32 values, the MSB is the sign bit (1 for negative, 0 for positive). For top-k largest, we want the largest values. Since IEEE 754 float32 has the property that (except for the sign bit), the integer comparison of the bit pattern matches the float comparison (for positive numbers), we can use bitwise radix select. The algorithm: 1. Xor all values with (sign_bit_mask) to convert to unsigned integers where ordering matches float ordering 2. Start with all n elements as candidates 3. For each bit from MSB to LSB: a. Count how many candidates have this bit set b. If count >= k, keep only those with bit set c. Else, keep all with bit set + (k - count) with bit clear d. Adjust k = max(0, k - count) if we kept all with bit set 4. After all bits, we have exactly k elements This is O(n * 32) but with very low constant factors. On GPU, each pass is a simple scan + partition. Actually, for GPU implementation, the radix select can be done more efficiently: - Use a single pass per bit - Use ballot intrinsics for the counting - Use shared memory for the partition This is getting complex. Let me think about what's the simplest implementation that will give good performance. **Simpler approach: Block-level top-k with shared memory bitonic sort** For each row: 1. Launch enough blocks to cover the row 2. Each block loads its chunk into shared memory as (value, index) pairs 3. Each block does a bitonic sort in shared memory 4. Each block writes its top-k to global memory 5. If multiple blocks, merge their results The shared memory bitonic sort for N elements with T threads: - Load N elements into shared memory (N pairs of float+int) - For bitonic sort: log2(N) * (log2(N) + 1) / 2 stages - Each stage does compare-and-swap For N=1024 elements per block: log2(1024) = 10 stages. 10 * 11 / 2 = 55 compare-swap passes. Each pass reads 2 elements, compares, writes back. With 1024 elements in shared memory, each pass takes O(N/2) comparisons. Total comparisons: 55 * 512 = 28160 per block. This is fast in shared memory. But for n=131072, we'd need 128 blocks per row, each producing 64 results. Then we need to merge 128 * 64 = 8192 candidates down to 64. That's another sort of 8192 elements. This approach works but the merge phase is expensive. **Better approach: Multi-pass block-level with intermediate merge** Actually, for n=131072 with k=64: - Use 128 blocks (1024 threads each, 1 element per thread per block for the first pass) - Actually no, 131072 elements / 1024 threads = 128, so each thread handles 1 element if we use 1 element per thread. Wait, that's not right. If we have 128 blocks, each with 1024 threads, each thread loads 1 element. Total: 128 * 1024 = 131072. Then each block sorts 1024 elements and outputs top 64. Then we have 128 * 64 = 8192 candidates. Sort those 8192 to get final top 64. For the merge, we could use a single block: load 8192 candidates, sort, output top 64. Total work: 128 blocks sort 1024 each, then 1 block sorts 8192. That's actually pretty efficient for a first pass. But 128 blocks is a lot of launches. We can do this in a single kernel with grid-stride loops, or use a two-kernel approach. **Let me simplify further:** Given the shapes, the most practical approach might be: 1. For rows where n <= 32768 (all except the first shape): one block per row, load into shared memory, bitonic sort, output top k. 2. For n = 131072: use a two-pass approach. First pass: multiple blocks per row, each loading a chunk. Actually, let me reconsider... For n = 131072, if I use 256 threads per block and each thread loads 2 elements (512 per block), I need 256 blocks. Each block sorts 512 elements and outputs top 64. Then merge 256 * 64 = 16384 candidates → sort → top 64. Hmm, that's 16384 candidates to merge which is still a lot. Let me try yet another approach: **Radix-select inspired approach using warp-level primitives:** For each row: 1. Each warp (32 threads) processes a chunk of the row 2. Within the warp, use __ballot_sync and __shfl_sync to do bitwise selection 3. Warps within a block cooperate via shared memory 4. Blocks merge via global memory This is getting too complex for a single kernel. Let me step back and think about what approach is both performant and implementable. **Final approach: Hybrid - block-level bitonic top-k + warp-level merge** The cleanest approach that handles all shapes well: For each row in the batch: - If n is small enough for one block: launch one block, sort in shared memory, output top k - If n needs multiple blocks: launch multiple blocks, each produces local top-k, then merge But actually, for the 3090, let me just go with a well-optimized **single-kernel bitonic top-k** approach: 1. Each block handles one row (or part of a row for the large case) 2. Load elements into registers 3. Within each warp, use warp shuffles to do a bitonic merge network 4. Between warps, use shared memory 5. Output top k Actually, let me look at what existing solutions do. Let me check if there's a known good approach for GPU top-k. The most common approach in production (e.g., in vLLM, FlashInfer, etc.) is: **Radix selection** or **Bucket-based selection**: - Quantize values to a histogram - Find the threshold bucket - Filter to elements above the threshold - If more than k, refine; if less, include from next bucket This is O(n) with low constant factors and works for any k. Let me go with a **histogram-based approach**: 1. Compute min and max of the input 2. Create a histogram with B buckets 3. From the histogram, find the threshold bucket where the cumulative count crosses k 4. Within the threshold bucket, do a more precise selection For fp32 values with a wide range, B=256 or B=1024 buckets should work well. Each bucket covers a range of values. Actually, the problem with histograms for fp32 is that values could be very clustered. If all values are in one bucket, we need a fallback. Let me try a different approach that I know works well: **parallel bitonic top-k in registers**. Here's the plan: **Register-based bitonic top-k kernel:** For each row, launch max(1, ceil(n / (BLOCK_SIZE * ITEMS_PER_THREAD))) blocks. Each block has BLOCK_SIZE threads (I'll use 256 or 512). Each thread loads ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements. Within each block, we use a combination of: 1. Register-level bitonic sort per thread (small, just ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements) 2. Warp-level bitonic merge network using __shfl_xor_sync 3. Block-level merge using shared memory After the block has its local top-k, we write to global memory. If multiple blocks per row, we need a merge kernel. For most shapes, one block per row is sufficient. Let me calculate: - n=131072, 256 threads: ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 131072/256 = 512. That's a LOT of registers. - Let me use ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 16 and multiple waves per block. Wait, I have a better idea. After each wave, the thread has its local top-k in registers, sorted 3. Warp-level merge of local top-k lists 4. After all waves: block has the top-k for its chunk 5. import reference, shapes
import torch
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    reference.batch = shape['batch']
    reference.n = shape['n']
    reference.k = shape['k']
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    x = reference.get_inputs()[0].to(device)
    model = reference.Model(*reference.get_init_inputs()).to(device).eval()
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(10):
        model(x)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(100):
        model(x)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 100
    bytes_moved = shape['batch'] * shape['n'] * 4 + shape['batch'] * shape['k'] * 12
    gbps = bytes_moved / (ms / 1000) / 1e9
    print(f'shape {shape}: {ms:.4f} ms, {gbps:.1f} GB/s')
"

import reference, shapes
import torch

device = torch.device('cuda:0')

for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    reference.batch = shape['batch']
    reference.n = shape['n']
    reference.k = shape['k']
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    x = reference.get_inputs()[0].to(device)
    model = reference.Model(*reference.get_init_inputs()).to(device).eval()
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(10):
        model(x)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(100):
        model(x)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 100
    bytes_moved = shape['batch'] * shape['n'] * 4 + shape['batch'] * shape['k'] * 12  # fp32 in + (fp32 val + int64 idx) out
    gbps = bytes_moved / (ms / 1000) / 1e9
    print(f'shape {shape}: {ms:.4f} ms, {gbps:.1f} GB/s ({gbps/936*100:.1f}% peak)')
"

(16, 12000, 16): Non-power-of-2 n. 5. (128, 4096, 1): Many rows, small n, k=1 (argmax). For shapes 2-5, n is small enough that a single block can handle each row. For shape 1, we need multiple blocks. **Simplified plan:** I'll implement a single CUDA kernel that uses a **bitonic sort network** in shared memory: For each row (handled by one or more blocks): 1. Load elements into shared memory as (value, index) pairs 2. Perform a bitonic sort in shared memory 3. Take the first k elements (they'll be sorted descending) For n <= 16384: single block per row. With ~40KB of shared memory: 40000 / 8 = 5000 elements per block. That's not enough for n=16384. But merging sorted lists efficiently on GPU is tricky. Actually, let me reconsider. The real bottleneck is reading the input from DRAM. If we can saturate DRAM bandwidth during the read, the computation is "free." So the key is to read the input efficiently and do minimal work. **Better approach: Radix-select kernel** The idea of radix select: 1. Iterate through bits of the float32 value (starting from MSB) 2. For each bit, count how many elements have this bit = 1 3. Partition: keep elements with the current bit = 1 if count >= k, otherwise keep all For GPU implementation: - Each block processes a chunk of the input - For each bit pass: read elements, count bits, partition into "selected" and "rejected" buckets - After all bit passes, we have exactly k elements This is essentially what torch.topk does internally (CUB radix select). But implementing a full radix select from scratch is complex. Let me think of a simpler approach. **Simplest viable approach: Parallel bitonic sort per row in shared memory** Given the relatively small n (max 131072), I can use this approach: For shape 1 (n=131072, batch=1, k=64): - Use 4 blocks of 256 threads each. Each block loads 32768 elements. - But 32768 elements at 8 bytes each is 256KB, won't fit in shared memory. - Alternative: Each thread loads 128 elements into registers, does a local sort, then warps merge. Actually, let me try a different angle. Let me focus on what's most practical: **Register-based warp-level bitonic sort + shared memory merge** This is the standard approach for efficient GPU sorting: 1. Each block handles a chunk of one row 2. Each thread loads ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements into registers 3. Each thread sorts its own elements (bitonic sort in registers, small network) 4. Warp-level merge: within each warp, threads exchange elements using __shfl_sync and merge 5. Block-level merge: warps exchange elements through shared memory 6. Output top k For the wave approach: if n doesn't fit in one pass, do multiple waves and merge. Let me code this up. I'll use 256 threads per block (good occupancy on 3090 with 48KB shared memory per block). Each thread loads a certain number of elements, we sort locally, then merge within the warp and across warps. Actually, I just realized that for k <= 64, I can use a much simpler approach: **Heap-based approach in registers:** - Each thread maintains a min-heap of k elements (the k largest seen so far) - Each thread scans through its chunk, inserting into the heap - This is O(n log k) per thread, which is efficient for small k But maintaining a heap in registers with 32/64 elements and doing it per-thread would be register-heavy. Let me think about this differently. The cleanest approach for these specific shapes: **Warp-level bitonic selection:** Use 32 threads per row (one warp) for shapes 2-5, and multiple warps for shape 1. Let me step back. **Simplest approach that works well:** I'll write a CUDA kernel that: 1. Uses one block per row (or multiple blocks for the large row) 2. Each block uses shared memory as a working buffer 3. Loads elements in tiles, does a bitonic sort on each tile, keeps top k 4. Merges the top-k from different tiles Actually, the SIMPLEST and most effective approach is: **Tiled block-level bitonic top-k in shared memory:** For each row (handled by one block with 256 threads): 1. Calculate how many elements we can fit in shared memory (e.g., 4096 elements = 32KB for fp32+int32 pairs) 2. For each tile: a. Load 4096 elements into shared memory b. Do bitonic sort (descending) on the 4096 elements in shared memory c. Take top k elements d. Merge with previously accumulated top-k (from previous tiles) 3. After all tiles, write final top-k The merge step is: given two sorted lists A and B (both size k), produce a sorted list C (size k) containing the k largest elements. This can be done efficiently in shared memory. For n=131072 and tile size 4096: 131072/4096 = 32 tiles. Each tile: sort 4096 elements, merge top-k. 32 * O(4096 log^2 4096) + 32 * O(k) work per block. With 256 threads and 4096 elements: each thread handles 16 elements during the sort. For n=8192: 8192/4096 = 2 tiles. Very fast. This approach handles non-power-of-2 n naturally (the last tile can be partially filled). Let me code this up. Bitonic sort in shared memory: - Requires power-of-2 number of elements. I'll pad to the next power of 2 for each tile. - For a bitonic sort of M elements: log2(M) * (log2(M) + 1) / 2 stages - M=4096: log2(4096) = 12, stages = 12*13/2 = 78. Each stage does M/2 comparisons. - Total: 78 * 2048 = 159,744 comparisons. With 256 threads, each does 624 comparisons. Very fast in shared memory. Wait, shared memory bitonic sort requires O(log^2 n) barrier synchronizations, which is 78 __syncthreads() calls. That's actually quite a few. But shared memory latency is ~20 cycles, so 78 barriers at maybe 1000 cycles each = 78,000 cycles ~= 50 microseconds at 1.4 GHz. That's significant. Alternative: use a more efficient shared memory sort. **Odd-even merge sort** (Batcher's) has the same complexity. Actually, for small k and large n, there's a much better approach: **Selection network + early pruning:** Instead of a full bitonic sort which is O(n log^2 n), use a selection network to extract the top k. But selection networks for arbitrary k are complex. **Bucket/Histogram approach:** 1. Find min and max of input values 2. Create B buckets (e.g., B=256) 3. Each thread scans its elements, increments histogram bucket counts 4. Prefix sum the histogram to find which bucket contains the k-th element 5. Filter: keep elements in buckets above the threshold 6. If the threshold bucket has many elements, refine within that bucket This is O(n) with low constant factors. Let me think more concretely. For fp32 values from randn, the range is roughly [-4, 4]. With 256 buckets over [-8, 8], each bucket covers 16/256 = 0.0625. If values are somewhat spread out, the threshold bucket might have ~n/256 elements, which could be hundreds or thousands. If we have too many elements in the threshold bucket, we can recurse: - Second pass with finer buckets within the threshold range - Or just sort the threshold bucket elements Let me try a different approach that I think will work well: **Bucket-based selection with bitonic fallback:** 1. Compute global min and max 2. Create 1024 buckets 3. Each thread scans its chunk, atomically increments bucket counts 4. Parallel prefix sum to compute bucket boundaries 5. Find threshold bucket T where cumulative count crosses k 6. If cumulative count up to bucket T-1 >= k: threshold is between T-1 and T 7. Count elements in the threshold bucket, and for elements above the threshold, write them directly to output Actually, implementing this with atomics and prefix sums on GPU is doable but requires careful engineering. Let me go with a simpler approach that I'm confident will work. **Final approach: Tiled shared-memory bitonic sort with merge** I'll implement this step by step: 1. CUDA kernel `topk_bitonic_kernel`: - Grid: (batch, num_blocks_per_row) - Block: 256 threads - Each block handles a chunk of one row - Shared memory: TILE_SIZE * sizeof(float2) for sorting buffer + k * sizeof(float2) for accumulator - Process tiles: for each tile, load into shared memory, bitonic sort, extract top k, merge with accumulator - After all tiles: write top k to global memory - If multiple blocks per row: write to a per-row buffer, then a merge kernel combines 2. For the multi-block case (n=131072), I'll use a two-phase approach: - Phase 1: multiple blocks each process a chunk, write top k to an intermediate buffer - Phase 2: single block merges all intermediate results Let me code this now. Actually wait, I want to think about what tile size is optimal. The RTX 3090 has 48KB of shared memory per SM (configurable up to 100KB by reducing L1). With 48KB, I can fit 48*1024/8 = 6144 elements per tile. I need it to be a power of 2 for the bitonic network. So 4096 elements (32KB) is the largest power-of-2 that fits comfortably, leaving 16KB for the accumulator and other data. With TILE_SIZE = 4096: - For n = 131072: 32 tiles per chunk - For n = 8192: 2 tiles - For n = 16384: 4 tiles - For n = 12000: ceil(12000/4096) = 3 tiles - For n = 4096: 1 tile Number of tiles per block is manageable. For n = 131072, using 4 blocks per row, each block handles 32768 elements = 8 tiles. Good. Let me now code the bitonic sort in shared memory. A bitonic sort of M = 2^m elements works as follows: - For stage = 1 to m: - For step = stage down to 1: - Compare elements distance 2^(step-1) apart, swap if needed Each stage-step pair requires a __syncthreads() barrier. In CUDA: ```cuda for (int stage = 2; stage <= M; stage *= 2) { for (int step = stage / 2; step > 0; step /= 2) { // Each thread compares two elements int tid = threadIdx.x; int i = tid + (tid / step) * step; // bit tricky to get right int j = i + step; // compare and swap __syncthreads(); } } ``` Actually, the standard CUDA bitonic sort pattern for shared memory is: ```cuda for (int size = 2; size <= M; size <<= 1) { // Bitonic merge sequence int dir = ((thread_id & (size / 2)) == 0) ? 1 : -1; // ascending/descending for (int stride = size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { int pos = thread_id + (thread_id / stride) * stride; int partner = pos ^ stride; // compare shared[pos] and shared[partner], swap if needed __syncthreads(); } } ``` Hmm, let me be careful about the indexing. For a bitonic sort in shared memory with T threads processing M elements: With T threads and M elements (M >= T), each thread handles M/T elements. This complicates the indexing. Simpler: Use M threads for the sort (M <= 1024 so this is feasible within a block). Each thread handles one element during the sort phase. But then we can only sort 1024 elements at a time. That's actually fine! Sort 1024 elements, extract top k, merge with accumulator. With k <= 64, we only need to process the accumulator merge a few times per tile of 1024. But with TILE_SIZE = 4096 and 1024 threads, we process 4096/1024 = 4 elements per thread during the load phase, but during the sort we'd need to map 4096 elements to 1024 threads. OK let me simplify. Let me use the standard bitonic sort approach where each thread handles one element during the sorting phase: Tile size: 1024 elements (8KB shared memory) Each thread: 1 element during sort For n=131072: 131072/1024 = 128 tiles per block (if 1 block) or 32 tiles per block (if 4 blocks) But 128 tiles with 78 stages each, each requiring __syncthreads(), that's 128 * 78 = ~10,000 barriers. That's way too many. Let me think about this again... **Better idea: register-based per-thread sort + shared memory merge** Instead of doing a full bitonic sort in shared memory, let each thread do its own sort in registers, then merge at warp and block level. Each thread processes ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements: 1. Load ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements into registers (pairs of float+int) 2. Sort these ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements using a register bitonic network (fast, no barriers) 3. Each thread now has a sorted list of ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements 4. Warp-level merge: using butterfly shuffle, each thread's sorted list merges with neighbor's 5. After warp merge: each warp has a sorted list of 32 * ITEMS_PER_THREAD elements distributed across its 32 threads 6. Block-level merge: warps merge their results through shared memory But step 4 and 5 are complex to implement correctly with variable-length sorted lists. **Even simpler: Per-thread heap + block-level merge** Each thread maintains a min-heap of k elements. For each element the thread sees: - If heap size < k: insert - Else if element > heap_min: replace min and heapify After processing all elements, each thread has its local top-k. Then: - Within each warp: merge 32 heaps of k elements each → warp top-k using shared memory - Across warps: merge warp top-k lists → block top-k using shared memory The heap operations are O(log k) each, and each thread processes n_total/T elements, so per-thread work is O(n_total/T * log k). For n=131072, T=256, k=64: 131072/256 * log2(64) = 512 * 6 = 3072 operations per thread. Very fast. The warp/block merge is O(WARPS * k * log(k * WARPS)) = O(8 * 64 * log 512) = O(8 * 64 * 9) = 4608 operations, also fast. This approach is simple and efficient! Let me code it up. Wait, there's a subtlety: maintaining a heap of k=64 elements in registers is 64 * 8 = 512 bytes per thread. RTX 3090 has 255 registers per thread maximum, each 4 bytes, so 1020 bytes of register space per thread. 512 bytes = 128 registers for the heap alone. Plus other variables. This is tight but should fit if we're careful. Actually, let me reconsider. 255 registers * 4 bytes = 1020 bytes per thread. With 256 threads per block: 256 * 1020 = 255KB register file per block. The SM has 256KB register file. So we can use 256 registers per thread with 256 threads per block, which is 256 * 256 * 4 = 256KB, just fitting. But 128 registers for the heap is a LOT. This would limit occupancy to 1 block per SM, which is bad for latency hiding. Alternative: store the heap in shared memory instead of registers. Each thread has its k-element heap in shared memory. With 256 threads and k=64: 256 * 64 * 8 = 128KB of shared memory. Way too much (max 48KB per block). Another alternative: only k=64 elements PER WARP instead of per thread. The warp cooperatively maintains a heap. But this requires warp-level coordination for each insertion, which is slow. **Another approach: Distributed top-k across threads** Each warp of 32 threads maintains the top k elements collectively: - The k elements are distributed across the 32 threads (each thread holds k/32 elements) - When a new element comes in, use warp shuffle to compare and potentially replace This is the approach used in CUB's BlockRadixSort and similar libraries. But implementation is complex. **Let me go with a pragmatic approach:** For these problem sizes, the simplest high-performance approach that handles all shapes: **Multi-pass block-level bitonic selective sort:** 1. Use shared memory buffer of 1024 elements (8KB) 2. Process tiles of 1024 elements: load into shared memory, sort, extract top k 3. Maintain a sorted list of accumulated top-k in shared memory 4. Merge each tile's top-k with the accumulator 5. Repeat until all elements processed 6. Write final result For sorting 1024 elements: use a bitonic sort with 1024 threads. 1024 elements = 2^10, so log2(1024) = 10, stages = 10*11/2 = 55. Each stage has a __syncthreads(). 55 barriers per sort. For n=131072 with 1 block: 128 tiles * 55 barriers = 7040 barriers. At ~1us per barrier, that's 7ms. Too slow. For n=131072 with 4 blocks: 32 tiles per block * 55 barriers = 1760 barriers. Still ~1.8ms. Better but still too much. What if I use a smaller tile, like 256 elements? - 256 = 2^8, stages = 8*9/2 = 36 barriers per sort - With 1024 threads and 256 elements: each thread handles exactly 1/4 element during sort. Need to map 4 virtual threads to 1 real thread. - n=131072 with 4 blocks: 32768/256 = 128 tiles * 36 = 4608 barriers. Still ~4.6ms. This barrier overhead is killing us. **Key insight: Don't do a full bitonic sort! Just select the top k!** For selecting top k << n, we don't need a full sort. We need selection. **Approach: Register-based tournament selection** 1. Each thread processes its chunk, maintaining the top k in a small register buffer 2. For each new element, compare with the k-th largest (smallest in the top-k), replace if larger 3. Keep the buffer sorted (using insertion sort or a bitonic merge for the new element) 4. After all elements: each thread has its local top-k (sorted) 5. Merge local top-k lists across threads For step 2-3, we need efficient insertion into a sorted list of k elements. For k=64, a binary search + shift takes O(k) per insertion, which is O(n * k / T) per thread. For n=131072, T=256, k=64: 131072/256 * 64 = 32768 comparisons and moves. That's fast. The bottleneck: merging 256 sorted lists of k elements each. Total input to merge: 256 * 64 = 16384 elements. Need to select top 64. For the merge, I can use shared memory: 1. 256 threads each write their sorted k elements to shared memory (but 256 * 64 * 8 = 128KB, doesn't fit) 2. Alternative: merge in a tree-like fashion. First, merge within warps. Then merge warp results. Warp-level merge (32 threads, each with k sorted elements): - Can use bitonic merge network or parallel merge - Result: 32 threads in the warp collectively have the top k from 32*k inputs Actually, the parallel merge is complex. Let me think of a simpler way. **Batch parallel selection within a warp:** Each warp handles its 32k elements to find the top k. Use a counting approach: - Each thread checks its k elements: for each element, count how many elements across the warp are larger - If the count < k, the element is in the top k - Use warp shuffle to exchange data This is O(32 * k^2) within a warp. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda #define TILE_SIZE 1024 // power of 2, fits in shared memory #define MAX_K 64 struct ValIdx { float value; int index; }; __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_values, long* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch_size, int n, int k ) { // Each block handles one row int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch_size) return; __shared__ ValIdx sort_buf[TILE_SIZE]; __shared__ ValIdx accum[MAX_K]; __shared__ int accum_size; if (threadIdx.x == 0) accum_size = 0; __syncthreads(); const float* row_input = input + row * n; int num_tiles = (n + TILE_SIZE - 1) / TILE_SIZE; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) { // Load tile into shared memory int global_idx = tile * TILE_SIZE + threadIdx.x; if (global_idx < n) { sort_buf[threadIdx.x].value = row_input[global_idx]; sort_buf[threadIdx.x].index = global_idx; } else { sort_buf[threadIdx.x].value = -INFINITY; sort_buf[threadIdx.x].index = -1; } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort (descending) for (int stage = 2; stage <= TILE_SIZE; stage <<= 1) { for (int step = stage >> 1; step > 0; step >>= 1) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int partner = tid ^ step; if (partner > tid) { bool ascending = ((tid & stage) == 0); ValIdx a = sort_buf[tid]; ValIdx b = sort_buf[partner]; bool swap = ascending ? (a.value > b.value) : (a.value < b.value); if (swap) { sort_buf[tid] = b; sort_buf[partner] = a; } } __syncthreads(); } } // Top k elements of this tile are at sort_buf[0..k-1] // Merge with accumulator // ... this is the complex part } } ``` Actually, merging with the accumulator after each tile is the tricky part. Let me try a fundamentally different approach that avoids sorting entirely for most elements. **Streaming top-k with warp-level min-heap:** Each warp maintains the top k elements in registers (as a sorted array). Each iteration: 1. Load 32 elements (one per thread in the warp) 2. Using warp shuffle, compare all 32 elements with the k-th largest 3. If any of the 32 is larger, merge into the top-k buffer The comparison: broadcast the k-th largest (min of top-k) to all threads. Each thread checks if its element > min. Use __ballot_sync to find which elements are candidates. Then insert candidates into the top-k buffer. This is similar to what CUB's BlockReduce does but for top-k. Actually, let me just use a well-known pattern. Here's what I think will work best: **Grid-stride loop with per-thread sorted buffer:** ```cuda // Each thread: float my_vals[k]; // top-k values, sorted descending int my_idxs[k]; // corresponding indices // Initialize with -INF for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { my_vals[i] = -INFINITY; my_idxs[i] = -1; } // Scan input with grid-stride loop for (int idx = global_tid; idx < n; idx += grid_stride) { float val = input[row * n + idx]; // Insert into sorted buffer if better than my_vals[k-1] if (val > my_vals[k-1]) { // Insert val at correct position, shift others down int pos = k - 1; while (pos > 0 && my_vals[pos-1] < val) { my_vals[pos] = my_vals[pos-1]; my_idxs[pos] = my_idxs[pos-1]; pos--; } my_vals[pos] = val; my_idxs[pos] = idx; } } // After scan: merge all threads' buffers into block-level top-k ``` The per-thread scan is O(n/grid_stride * k). With k=64, 2M elements, grid_stride=65536: each thread processes ~30 elements * 64 comparisons = ~2000 operations. Very fast. The block-level merge: we have 256 threads, each with k sorted elements. Let me just code this up now! ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #define MAX_K 64 // Merge two sorted descending arrays of size k, result in a __device__ void merge_k(float* a_val, int* a_idx, const float* b_val, const int* b_idx, int k) { float tmp_val[MAX_K]; int tmp_idx[MAX_K]; int i = 0, j = 0, out = 0; while (out < k) { if (a_val[i] >= b_val[j]) { tmp_val[out] = a_val[i]; tmp_idx[out] = a_idx[i]; i++; } else { tmp_val[out] = b_val[j]; tmp_idx[out] = b_idx[j]; j++; } out++; } for (int x = 0; x < k; x++) { a_val[x] = tmp_val[x]; a_idx[x] = tmp_idx[x]; } } __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_values, long* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch_size, int n, int k ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch_size) return; const float* row_input = input + row * n; // Per-thread top-k buffer (registers) float my_val[MAX_K]; int my_idx[MAX_K]; // Initialize #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) { my_val[i] = -INFINITY; my_idx[i] = -1; } // Scan chunk int tid = threadIdx.x; int stride = blockDim.x; for (int idx = tid; idx < n; idx += stride) { float val = row_input[idx]; // Insert into sorted buffer if (val > my_val[k-1]) { int pos = k - 1; while (pos > 0 && my_val[pos-1] < val) { my_val[pos] = my_val[pos-1]; my_idx[pos] = my_idx[pos-1]; pos--; } my_val[pos] = val; my_idx[pos] = idx; } } // ... warp merge ... // ... block merge ... // ... write output ... } ``` Wait, I'm using MAX_K for the array size but k is a runtime parameter. I need to be careful: I declared arrays of size MAX_K but only use the first k elements. For initializing, I should initialize all MAX_K elements. For the insertion, I compare against my_val[k-1] which only works if k <= MAX_K. This is fine, but I need to handle the case where k < MAX_K. The unused elements should remain at -INFINITY. For the merge, I pass k as parameter. OK let me also think about a critical optimization: the insertion into the sorted buffer is O(k). I can make it faster by using binary search to find the insertion point, then memmove the tail. ```cuda // Binary search insertion point int lo = 0, hi = k; while (lo < hi) { int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1; if (my_val[mid] < val) hi = mid; else lo = mid + 1; } // lo is insertion point // Shift elements from lo to k-2 down by 1 for (int i = k - 1; i > lo; i--) { my_val[i] = my_val[i-1]; my_idx[i] = my_idx[i-1]; } if (lo < k) { my_val[lo] = val; my_idx[lo] = idx; } ``` This uses binary search (O(log k) comparisons) + shift (O(k) moves). The moves are just register copies and should compile to efficient code. For k=64, binary search does ~6 comparisons, shift does up to 64 register copies. Much better than the linear search approach which does up to 64 comparisons + 64 shifts. Now, for the warp merge: ```cuda // Warp merge: butterfly shuffle int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5; for (int mask = 1; mask < 32; mask <<= 1) { float partner_val[MAX_K]; int partner_idx[MAX_K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) { partner_val[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[i], mask); partner_idx[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[i], mask); } merge_k(my_val, my_idx, partner_val, partner_idx, k); } ``` This does 5 rounds of merge. After this, all 32 threads in the warp have the same top-k (warp-level top-k). Then for block merge: ```cuda __shared__ float block_val[MAX_K * 32]; // 32 warps max? No, 8 warps with 256 threads __shared__ int block_idx[MAX_K * 32]; ``` Wait, 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps. Each warp contributes k elements. Total: 8 * k <= 8 * 64 = 512 elements. I can store 512 elements in shared memory and do a bitonic sort, or merge sequentially. For the block merge, I'll have thread 0 from each warp write to shared memory, then sort. But we need a barrier between the warp merge (shuffle-based, no barrier needed) and the write to shared memory. Actually, warp shuffle is synchronized within the warp, but across warps there's no guarantee. I need a __syncthreads() before writing to shared memory. Let me finalize: ```cuda __global__ void topk_kernel(...) { // ... per-thread scan ... // Warp merge for (int mask = 1; mask < 32; mask <<= 1) { // exchange and merge } // After warp merge: only lane 0 of each warp has the canonical result // (Actually all lanes have the same result after butterfly merge) __syncthreads(); // ensure all warps done __shared__ float merge_val[MAX_K * 8]; // 8 warps * MAX_K __shared__ int merge_idx[MAX_K * 8]; // Thread 0 of each warp writes if (lane == 0) { for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { merge_val[warp_id * MAX_K + i] = my_val[i]; merge_idx[warp_id * MAX_K + i] = my_idx[i]; } } __syncthreads(); // Now merge: take top k from num_warps * k elements // ... bitonic sort or sequential merge ... } ``` For merging the block-level elements: I can use a bitonic sort on `num_warps * k` elements (padded to next power of 2) in shared memory. Or I can do a simpler sequential merge using thread 0. Actually, for k=64 and 8 warps: 512 elements. Bitonic sort of 512 elements: 9*10/2 = 45 stages. Each stage needs __syncthreads(). +"""Top-k via register-based insertion + warp butterfly merge + block bitonic merge.
+
+Design:
+  - One block per row (256 threads). For n > 32768, multiple blocks per row
+    scan disjoint chunks; a second merge kernel combines them.
+  - Each thread scans its chunk, maintaining a sorted-descending buffer of k
+    (value, index) pairs in registers. Insertion uses binary search + shift.
+  - Warp merge: butterfly shuffle (__shfl_xor_sync) exchanges and merges
+    buffers between thread pairs; after log2(32) rounds every lane holds the
+    warp-wide top-k.
+  - Block merge: warp-0 threads write their (identical) results to shared
+    memory, then a bitonic sort extracts the block-wide top-k.
+  - For single-block rows the block result goes straight to the output.
+    Multi-block rows write to an intermediate buffer and a second kernel merges.
+"""
+
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import os
+from pathlib import Path
+from typing import List
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+OP_TYPE = "topk"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# CUDA source
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+_cuda_src = r"""
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cfloat.h>
+
+#define MAX_K 64
+#define WARP_SIZE 32
+#define BLOCK_SIZE 256
+#define NUM_WARPS (BLOCK_SIZE / WARP_SIZE)   // 8
+#define MERGE_BUF_SIZE 512                   // NUM_WARPS * MAX_K, power of 2
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Device helpers
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+// Merge two sorted-descending arrays A and B (each of length k) into A,
+// keeping only the largest k elements.
+__device__ __forceinline__ void merge_two_sorted(
+    float* __restrict__ a_val, int* __restrict__ a_idx,
+    const float* __restrict__ b_val, const int* __restrict__ b_idx,
+    int k)
+{
+    float tmp_val[MAX_K];
+    int   tmp_idx[MAX_K];
+    int i = 0, j = 0, out = 0;
+    #pragma unroll 1
+    while (out < k) {
+        bool take_a;
+        if (i >= k)      take_a = false;
+        else if (j >= k) take_a = true;
+        else             take_a = (a_val[i] >= b_val[j]);
+        if (take_a) {
+            tmp_val[out] = a_val[i];
+            tmp_idx[out] = a_idx[i];
+            i++;
+        } else {
+            tmp_val[out] = b_val[j];
+            tmp_idx[out] = b_idx[j];
+            j++;
+        }
+        out++;
+    }
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int x = 0; x < MAX_K; x++) {
+        if (x < k) {
+            a_val[x] = tmp_val[x];
+            a_idx[x] = tmp_idx[x];
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Scan kernel: per-row top-k scan with register buffers
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+extern "C" __global__ void topk_scan_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ input,       // (batch, n)
+    float* __restrict__ out_values,        // (batch, k)  — final output when blocks_per_row==1
+    long* __restrict__ out_indices,        // (batch, k)
+    float* __restrict__ inter_values,      // (batch, blocks_per_row, k) — intermediate
+    int* __restrict__   inter_indices,     // (batch, blocks_per_row, k)
+    int batch_size,
+    int n,
+    int k,
+    int blocks_per_row
+) {
+    int row       = blockIdx.x / blocks_per_row;
+    int block_col = blockIdx.x % blocks_per_row;
+
+    if (row >= batch_size) return;
+
+    // --- Per-thread top-k buffer (registers) ---
+    float my_val[MAX_K];
+    int   my_idx[MAX_K];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) {
+        my_val[i] = -FLT_MAX;
+        my_idx[i] = -1;
+    }
+
+    // Chunk this block is responsible for
+    int chunk_start = block_col * (n / blocks_per_row);
+    int chunk_end   = (block_col == blocks_per_row - 1) ? n
+                     : (block_col + 1) * (n / blocks_per_row);
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+
+    // --- Scan chunk: grid-stride over the chunk ---
+    for (int idx = chunk_start + tid; idx < chunk_end; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) {
+        float val = __ldg(input + row * n + idx);
+        // Early reject against the smallest in the top-k buffer
+        if (val <= my_val[k - 1]) continue;
+
+        // Binary-search insertion point (descending order)
+        int lo = 0, hi = k - 1;
+        #pragma unroll 1
+        while (lo < hi) {
+            int mid = (lo + hi) >> 1;
+            if (my_val[mid] < val) hi = mid;
+            else                   lo = mid + 1;
+        }
+        int pos = lo;
+        // Shift tail down by 1
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = k - 1; i > pos; i--) {
+            my_val[i] = my_val[i - 1];
+            my_idx[i] = my_idx[i - 1];
+        }
+        my_val[pos] = val;
+        my_idx[pos] = idx;
+    }
+
+    // --- Warp-level butterfly merge ---
+    for (int mask = 1; mask < WARP_SIZE; mask <<= 1) {
+        float partner_val[MAX_K];
+        int   partner_idx[MAX_K];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < MAX_K; i++) {
+            partner_val[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[i], mask);
+            partner_idx[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[i], mask);
+        }
+        merge_two_sorted(my_val, my_idx, partner_val, partner_idx, k);
+    }
+
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    // --- Block-level merge in shared memory ---
+    // After warp merge, every lane within a warp has identical data.
+    // Lane 0 of each warp writes to shared memory.
+    int lane    = tid & 31;
+    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
+
+    __shared__ floa

1	"""Top-k via register-based insertion + warp butterfly merge + block bitonic merge.
2	
3	Design:
4	  - One block per row (256 threads). For n > 32768, multiple blocks per row
5	    scan disjoint chunks; a second merge kernel combines them.
6	  - Each thread scans its chunk, maintaining a sorted-descending buffer of k
7	    (value, index) pairs in registers. Insertion uses binary search + shift.
8	  - Warp merge: butterfly shuffle (__shfl_xor_sync) exchanges and merges
9	    buffers between thread pairs; after log2(32) rounds every lane holds the
10	    warp-wide top-k.
11	  - Block merge: warp-0 threads write their (identical) results to shared
12	    memory, then a bitonic sort extracts the block-wide top-k.
13	  - For single-block rows the block result goes straight to the output.
14	    Multi-block rows write to an intermediate buffer and a second kernel merges.
15	"""
16	
17	from __future__ import annotations
18	
19	import os
20	from pathlib import Path
21	from typing import List
22	
23	import torch
24	import torch.nn as nn
25	from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
26	
27	OP_TYPE = "topk"
28	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
29	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]

31	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
32	# CUDA source

This suggests a fundamental bug in the kernel logic. Let me think about what could be wrong: 1. **Chunk handling**: The chunk_start calculation `block_col * (n / blocks_per_row)` might have issues. For n=131072 with blocks_per_row=1, chunk_start=0, chunk_end=n=131072. That seems correct. 2. **Warp merge**: After the warp merge, all lanes should have the same data. But the bitonic merge at the block level uses `merge_val[base + i] = my_val[i]` where `base = warp_id * MAX_K`. If each warp contributes 64 elements, and we have 8 warps, that's 512 elements. The sort then finds the top k from these 512. 3. **Bitonic sort direction**: The comment says "descending" but let me verify the ascending/descending logic. Looking at the bitonic sort: ```cuda bool ascending = ((i & stage) == 0); bool swap = ascending ? (a_val > b_val) : (a_val < b_val); ``` In a bitonic sort, the `ascending` flag controls whether we're building an ascending or descending sequence at each stage. The pattern `((i & stage) == 0)` toggles between ascending and descending blocks. For a descending sort, we want the final result to be descending (largest first). In the standard bitonic sort: - Stage 2, 4, 8, ... 512 - For each stage, we have blocks of size `stage` - Blocks alternate between ascending and descending The test `((i & stage) == 0)` means: if the `stage` bit of `i` is 0, the block is ascending; if 1, it's descending. This is the standard pattern. When `ascending = true`: we want `a_val <= b_val` (a comes before b = smaller first). So we swap if `a_val > b_val`. When `ascending = false`: we want `a_val >= b_val` (larger first). So we swap if `a_val < b_val`. This matches my code. But wait — I want the final result to be **descending** (largest first). Is the final block ascending or descending? For a full bitonic sort of size M, the final block (stage = M) is: - `i & M` == 0 for i < M (since M is a power of 2 and i < M) - So `ascending = true` for all elements - Swap if `a_val > b_val` - This means the entire array is sorted ascending But I want descending! So I need to either invert the direction or reverse at the end. Wait, let me re-examine. The standard bitonic sort produces an ascending sequence. But I want descending (largest first). I should either: 1. Change `ascending = ((i & stage) != 0)` to produce descending first 2. Or reverse the comparison: `ascending ? (a_val < b_val) : (a_val > b_val)` 3. Or just read from the end of the sorted array (merge_val[MERGE_BUF_SIZE - 1 - tid]) Actually, looking at my output write: ```cuda out_values[row * k + tid] = merge_val[tid]; ``` I'm reading elements 0..k-1 from the sorted array. If the sort produces ascending order, then `merge_val[0]` is the SMALLEST, not the largest. So I need to either: - Fix the sort to produce descending order - Read from the end: `merge_val[MERGE_BUF_SIZE - 1 - tid]` Let me fix the sort to produce descending order. For stage=4, blocks of size 4, etc. The test `(i & stage) == 0` checks if the block starting at position i (where blocks are of size `stage`) is even or odd. For blocks of size `stage`, the block number is `i / stage`, and `i & stage` checks if bit log2(stage) is set in i, which is equivalent to `(i / stage) % 2 == 1`. """Centralized GPU timing for benchmark.py files.

All problems' benchmark.py should call `time_fn` from here so we have one
implementation of warmup, L2 flush, and CUDA event capture to maintain.

Methodology:
  - 10 warmup calls absorb Triton autotune (typical ~7 configs) and
    torch.compile reduce-overhead CUDA-graph capture.
  - Between each timed call, `_l2_flush()` writes 128 MB to evict L2
    (Blackwell L2 is 96 MB; we want any prior L2 contents flushed so
    we measure HBM-load bandwidth, not L2-cached bandwidth).
  - GPU timing via cuda.Event with synchronize() AFTER record() but
    BEFORE elapsed_time() — the canonical NVIDIA pattern.
  - Reported value is the median of `iters` trials, robust to outliers.

Notes / known biases not addressed here:
  - torch.compile(mode="reduce-overhead") gets CUDA graphs which eliminate
    launch overhead. Custom Triton/CUDA kernels do NOT get this treatment.
    On small shapes where launch overhead matters, this gives compile an
    artificial advantage. We accept this as the cost of using the agreed
    "compiled" baseline policy.
  - cuBLAS / cuDNN allocate workspaces on first call. 10 warmup absorbs.
  - Median over a fairly small number of trials (default 30) is fine for
    headline numbers but won't catch bimodal distributions. Use --extra
    instrumentation if you ever care.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import os
import re
import statistics
import time
from datetime import UTC, datetime

import torch

            variant,
            elapsed_s=elapsed_s,
            error=type(exc).__name__,
        )
        raise
    elapsed_s = f"{time.perf_counter() - start:.3f}"
    emit_benchmark_event("variant_end", shape_idx, variant, elapsed_s=elapsed_s, ms=f"{ms:.6f}")
    return ms


def time_fn(fn, inputs, iters: int = 30, warmup: int = 10) -> float:
    """Time `fn(*inputs)` and return the median wall time in milliseconds.

    Each timed call is preceded by an L2 flush.
    """
    for _ in range(warmup):
        with torch.no_grad():
            fn(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()

    times: list[float] = []
    for _ in range(iters):
        _l2_flush()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        with torch.no_grad():
            fn(*inputs)
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(times)

Estimate a threshold (e.g., using sampling) 2. Count elements above threshold 3. If count > k, raise threshold; if count < k, lower threshold 4. After finding the right threshold, filter and sort the k elements above it But this requires multiple passes. **Even better optimization: Radix-select approach** This is what torch.topk uses. Instead of sorting or insertion, use bitwise radix selection: 1. For each bit from MSB (31) to LSB (0): a. Count how many elements have this bit set b. Partition: keep elements above threshold bit pattern 2. After all bits, we have exactly k elements This is O(n) per pass × 32 passes, but each pass is a simple count + partition. On GPU, this can be done with warp-level ballot operations. But implementing a full radix select is complex. Just append new elements and periodically sort. Actually, for k=64, the simplest optimization is: **Use a bitonic selection network instead of binary search + shift** Instead of binary search + O(k) shift, use a bitonic merge to insert one element into a sorted list: 1. Append the new element at the end (position k) 2. Do a bitonic merge of the k+1 elements to extract the top k The bitonic merge of k+1 elements takes O(log k) steps. For k=64: log2(64) = 6 steps, each doing a few comparisons. This is much faster than the O(k) shift. -"""Top-k via register-based insertion + warp butterfly merge + block bitonic merge.
-
-Design:
-  - One block per row (256 threads). For n > 32768, multiple blocks per row
-    scan disjoint chunks; a second merge kernel combines them.
-  - Each thread scans its chunk, maintaining a sorted-descending buffer of k
-    (value, index) pairs in registers. Insertion uses binary search + shift.
-  - Warp merge: butterfly shuffle (__shfl_xor_sync) exchanges and merges
-    buffers between thread pairs; after log2(32) rounds every lane holds the
-    warp-wide top-k.
-  - Block merge: warp-0 threads write their (identical) results to shared
-    memory, then a bitonic sort extracts the block-wide top-k.
-  - For single-block rows the block result goes straight to the output.
-    Multi-block rows write to an intermediate buffer and a second kernel merges.

+"""Top-k via register-based heap scan + shared-memory warp/block merge.
+
+Design (v2 — register-spill fix):
+  - Each thread scans its chunk, maintaining a MIN-HEAP of k elements in
+    registers.  Only 2k registers (k floats + k ints).  Insertion is O(log k).
+  - After scan: sort the heap in registers → sorted descending top-k.
+  - Warp merge: write to shared memory, merge within warp via shared memory
+    (avoids register-spilling partner arrays).
+  - Block merge: bitonic sort of warp results in shared memory.
+  - Multi-block paths write to an intermediate buffer; a merge kernel combines.
 """

+// Push val,idx onto a min-heap of size k stored in h_val/h_idx.
+// h_val[0] is the minimum (k-th largest seen so far).
+__device__ __forceinline__ void heap_push(
+    float* h_val, int* h_idx, int k, float val, int idx)
+{
+    if (val <= h_val[0]) return;   // not better than current k-th largest
+    // Replace root and sift down
+    int pos = 0;
+    while (1) {
+        int left  = 2 * pos + 1;
+        int right = 2 * pos + 2;
+        int smallest = pos;
+
+        if (left  < k && h_val[left]  < h_val[smallest]) smallest = left;
+        if (right < k && h_val[right] < h_val[smallest]) smallest = right;
+
+        if (smallest == pos) {
+            h_val[pos] = val;
+            h_idx[pos] = idx;
+            break;
+        }
+        h_val[pos] = h_val[smallest];
+        h_idx[pos] = h_idx[smallest];
+        pos = smallest;
+    }
+}
+
+// Sort a min-heap of size k into descending order using heap-sort.
+__device__ __forceinline__ void heap_sort_descending(
+    float* h_val, int* h_idx, int k)
+{
+    // Repeatedly extract min to build ascending order at the end,
+    // then reverse in-place for descending.
+    for (int end = k - 1; end > 0; end--) {

+        float tmp_v  = h_val[0];

+        h_val[0]     = h_val[end];

+        h_val[end]   = tmp_v;
+        h_idx[end]   = tmp_i;
+
+        // Sift down new root
+        int pos = 0;
+        while (1) {
+            int left  = 2 * pos + 1;
+            int right = 2 * pos + 2;
+            int smallest = pos;
+            if (left  < end && h_val[left]  < h_val[smallest]) smallest = left;
+            if (right < end && h_val[right] < h_val[smallest]) smallest = right;

+            h_val[smallest] = sv; h_idx[smallest] = si;
+            pos = smallest;
+        }
+    }
+    // Now h_val[0..k-1] is ascending; reverse to descending
+    for (int i = 0; i < k / 2; i++) {
+        float sv = h_val[i]; int si = h_idx[i];
+        h_val[i] = h_val[k - 1 - i]; h_idx[i] = h_idx[k - 1 - i];
+        h_val[k - 1 - i] = sv; h_idx[k - 1 - i] = si;
+    }
+}
