Let me wait for the CUDA_LAUNCH_BLOCKING output. But rather than waiting, let me think about the bug while it runs. Looking at my v2 kernel, the potential issue is in the heap fill phase: ```cuda // First, fill the heap with the first k elements int first_k_end = min(chunk_start + k, chunk_end); int fill_idx = chunk_start + tid; for (int i = 0; i < k && fill_idx < chunk_end; i++, fill_idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { ... } ``` This loop: `fill_idx` starts at `chunk_start + tid`. Then increments by `BLOCK_SIZE` (256). After `k` iterations (64 iterations for k=64), `fill_idx` could go way past `chunk_end`. The loop condition is `i < k && fill_idx < chunk_end`, but `fill_idx` can jump past `chunk_end` in one iteration and then the condition fails. Wait, let me trace through for shape 0 (n=131072, k=64): - chunk_start = 0, chunk_end = 131072 - fill_idx for thread 0: 0, 256, 512, 768, ... (up to 63 * 256 = 16128) - All are < 131072, so the loop loads 64 elements for thread 0 - fill_idx for thread 255: 255, 511, 767, ... (up to 255 + 63*256 = 16383) - All are < 131072, so fine But then after the fill phase, I have: ```cuda // Number of elements actually in heap int heap_size = min(k, (chunk_end - chunk_start + BLOCK_SIZE - 1) / BLOCK_SIZE); ``` This is `min(64, (131072 + 255) / 256) = min(64, 513) = 64`. So heap_size = k = 64. Good. Then the push phase: ```cuda for (int idx = chunk_start + k + tid; idx < chunk_end; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float val = __ldg(input + row * n + idx); heap_push(my_val, my_idx, k, val, idx); } ``` Wait, the initial `idx = chunk_start + k + tid`. This starts at position `k + tid`. But the fill phase already loaded `chunk_start + tid + i*BLOCK_SIZE` for i=0..k-1. So the fill phase covers positions: tid, tid+256, tid+512, ..., tid+(k-1)*256. The push phase starts at `k + tid`. For thread 0: idx starts at 64. But the fill phase already loaded positions 0, 256, 512, ..., 16128. Position 64 was NOT loaded by thread 0, but it was loaded by thread 64 (tid=64 loads position 64). So each position is loaded exactly once: either by the fill phase (one thread) or the push phase (another thread). This is correct. But wait — there's a subtle bug: the fill phase loads elements at positions `chunk_start + tid + i * BLOCK_SIZE`. For thread 255 with i=63: position = 0 + 255 + 63*256 = 16383. The push phase for thread 255 starts at `0 + 64 + 255 = 319`. But position 255 was already loaded in iteration i=0, and position 511 was loaded in i=1, etc. The push phase for thread 255 loads positions 319, 575, 831, ... which are all different from the fill phase positions. OK so the element distribution is correct. But there's a bug in the initial heap construction: The heap is built using bottom-up insertion (sift up). For the first k elements loaded by each thread, they're inserted sequentially. But the positions loaded by different threads overlap in value range. The heap construction assumes the first k elements form a valid heap, which they should after the sift-up insertions. Actually wait, there's another issue. The initial heap construction loop: ```cuda int fill_idx = chunk_start + tid; for (int i = 0; i < k && fill_idx < chunk_end; i++, fill_idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { ... int pos = i; while (pos > 0) { int parent = (pos - 1) / 2; if (my_val[parent] <= val) break; my_val[pos] = my_val[parent]; my_idx[pos] = my_idx[parent]; pos = parent; } my_val[pos] = val; my_idx[pos] = fill_idx; } ``` This inserts k elements, building a min-heap. The heap at index i has i+1 elements. After inserting all k elements, we have a valid min-heap of k elements. This is correct. But the sift-up loop uses `my_val[parent]` where parent = (pos-1)/2. Initially, my_val is initialized to NEG_INF for all positions. So for the first element (i=0), pos starts at 0, sift-up loop doesn't execute, and val is placed at position 0. For the second element (i=1), parent = 0, my_val[0] is the first element, compare and swap if needed. This seems correct. Let me check if there's an issue with the fill phase not filling all k positions... Actually, I think the bug might be that for some threads, `fill_idx` exceeds `chunk_end` before i reaches k. For threads with high tid: For shape (1, 131072, 64): - Thread 255: fill_idx = 255, 511, 767, ..., 255 + 63*256 = 16383 - chunk_end = 131072 - All 64 iterations succeed (16383 < 131072) But for shape (16, 12000, 16): - chunk_start = 0, chunk_end = 12000 - Thread 255: fill_idx = 255, 511, 767, ..., 255 + 15*256 = 4095 - 4095 < 12000, so all 16 iterations succeed. OK. But for shape (128, 4096, 1): - chunk_start = 0, chunk_end = 4096 - k = 1, so the fill loop runs once - Thread 255: fill_idx = 255 < 4096. OK. Hmm, all shapes should be fine. Let me think about what else could cause an illegal memory access... Oh wait! I think I see the issue. The heap_push function modifies my_val and my_idx, but the fill phase doesn't use heap_push — it does a manual sift-up. The heap_push function assumes a valid min-heap of size k, and tries to push a new element. But heap_push is called AFTER the fill phase, when the heap is full. Looking at heap_push: ```cuda __device__ __forceinline__ void heap_push( float* h_val, int* h_idx, int k, float val, int idx) { if (val <= h_val[0]) return; int pos = 0; while (1) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; ... ``` This accesses `h_val[left]` and `h_val[right]` where left and right can be ≥ k. It checks `left < k` before using them, so this should be fine. Wait, let me check the heap_sort_descending function more carefully: ```cuda __device__ __forceinline__ void heap_sort_descending( float* h_val, int* h_idx, int k) { for (int end = k - 1; end > 0; end--) { // Swap root with end float tmp_v = h_val[0]; int tmp_i = h_idx[0]; h_val[0] = h_val[end]; h_idx[0] = h_idx[end]; h_val[end] = tmp_v; h_idx[end] = tmp_i; // Sift down new root int pos = 0; while (1) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < end && h_val[left] < h_val[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < end && h_val[right] < h_val[smallest]) smallest = right; ... ``` This accesses elements up to position `2*pos+2` which can be up to `2*(end-2)+2 = 2*end-2` when pos is near end. But it checks `left < end` and `right < end` before accessing. So this should be safe too. Hmm. Let me look at the warp merge shared memory: ```cuda // Write to warp_sm_val[0][lane] for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { warp_sm_val[0][lane][i] = my_val[i]; warp_sm_idx[0][lane][i] = my_idx[i]; } ``` The shared memory arrays are: ```cuda __shared__ float warp_sm_val[2][WARP_SIZE][MAX_K]; __shared__ int warp_sm_idx[2][WARP_SIZE][MAX_K]; ``` That's 2 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 16KB for `warp_sm_val` and 2 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 16KB for `warp_sm_idx`. Total: 32KB shared memory. Plus block merge: 512 * 4 * 2 = 4KB. Total: 36KB. That fits in 48KB. But wait, the indices of `warp_sm_val[2][WARP_SIZE][MAX_K]` — lane goes up to 31, i goes up to 63. The total size is 2 * 32 * 64 = 4096 elements per array. So accessing `warp_sm_val[0][lane][i]` where lane < 32 and i < 64 is valid. But what if `merge_two_sorted_smem` accesses `warp_sm_val[src][partner][i]` where partner is the result of `lane ^ mask`? That's still < 32, so it's valid. Wait, `merge_two_sorted_smem` uses the SAME shared memory array for its temp storage: ```cuda __device__ __forceinline__ void merge_two_sorted_smem( float* __restrict__ a_val, int* __restrict__ a_idx, const float* __restrict__ b_val, const int* __restrict__ b_idx, int k, float* sm_tmp_val, int* sm_tmp_idx, int lane) { int base = lane * MAX_K; ... sm_tmp_val[base + out] = ...; ... ``` And it's called as: ```cuda merge_two_sorted_smem( warp_sm_val[src][lane], warp_sm_idx[src][lane], warp_sm_val[src][partner], warp_sm_idx[src][partner], k, warp_sm_val[dst][lane], warp_sm_idx[dst][lane], lane); ``` So the temp storage is `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]`, where dst is either 0 or 1. And the base is `lane * MAX_K`. But `warp_sm_val[dst]` is `float[MAX_K][WARP_SIZE]`, which is 32*64 elements. `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]` is a pointer to `warp_sm_val[dst][lane][0]`, which is a float[64] array. So `sm_tmp_val[base + out]` accesses `warp_sm_val[dst][lane][out]` where out < k ≤ 64. This is valid. But wait, `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]` is the third dimension, and I'm passing `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]` to `merge_two_sorted_smem` which treats it as a flat array and uses `base = lane * MAX_K` to index into it... Oh! There's the bug! `merge_two_sorted_smem` does: ```cuda int base = lane * MAX_K; sm_tmp_val[base + out] = ...; ``` But `sm_tmp_val` is `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]`, which is `float[64]` (a single lane's slot of 64 floats). So `sm_tmp_val[base + out]` accesses `sm_tmp_val[lane * 64 + out]`, but `sm_tmp_val` only has 64 elements (indices 0..63)! The issue: I passed `warp_sm_val[dst][lane]` to the function, which points to a 64-element array. But the function computes `base = lane * MAX_K` and then accesses `sm_tmp_val[base + out]` = `sm_tmp_val[lane * 64 + out]`, which is out of bounds for lane > 0. The fix: I should pass the base pointer `warp_sm_val[dst]` instead, which points to the full [32][64] array. Let me fix this. The function signature should take `float* sm_tmp_val` pointing to the entire [32][64] array, not just one lane's slot.

This is correct! The butterfly merge with shared memory produces the right results. But the number of __syncthreads() is 0 within the warp merge loop, which is correct since all threads are in the same warp and use warp-synchronous access to shared memory. These are different memory locations — no race. Good, the design seems correct. But I realized another issue: after the merge, I need to swap `src` and `dst`: ```cuda src = dst; dst = 1 - src; ``` This alternates src between 0 and 1. Wait, I'm passing `WSM_VAL(dst, 0)` to `merge_two_sorted_smem` as the temp buffer. But the temp buffer needs to be sized to hold data for ALL lanes (since `base = lane * MAX_K` inside the function). `WSM_VAL(dst, 0)` points to the START of the dst buffer, which is `warp_sm_val + dst * WARP_SIZE * MAX_K`. This has room for `WARP_SIZE * MAX_K = 32 * 64 = 2048` elements. And the function writes up to `lane * MAX_K + k` elements = up to `31 * 64 + 64 = 2048` elements. Actually, within a warp, each lane executes independently but all lanes share the same instruction pointer. Wait, this is wrong. Within a warp, shared memory writes from one lane ARE visible to other lanes within the same warp immediately (well, after the write instruction completes, which is in-order within the warp). So if lane 0 reads from src_buf[2] while lane 2 is writing to src_buf[2], there's a race condition. To avoid this, I need to ensure no lane writes to a location that another lane reads in the same round. The standard approach: use TWO buffers and swap. Actually, wait. In the butterfly merge, lane i reads from src[i] and src[i^mask]. Lane i writes to dst[i]. Lane i^mask reads from src[i^mask] and src[i]. Lane i^mask writes to dst[i^mask]. OK, the per-thread insertion looks correct. Let me check the bitonic sort more carefully. The bitonic sort in the block merge: ```cuda bool ascending = ((i & stage) != 0); ``` With this, the final sort is DESCENDING (largest first). Let me verify: For MERGE_BUF_SIZE=512, stage goes: 2, 4, 8, ..., 512. At stage=512: i ranges from 0 to 511. `i & 512` is always 0 (since i < 512). So `ascending = false` for all elements. For `ascending = false`, the comparison is `a_val < b_val` → swap if a_val < b_val. This means larger values go to lower indices (since we swap when a < b). Result: descending. But wait, at lower stages (2, 4, 8, ...), the ascending flag toggles based on `(i & stage) != 0`. This creates alternating ascending/descending blocks of size `stage`. This is the correct bitonic sort pattern for building bitonic sequences. To get a descending final result, the highest stage (stage = MERGE_BUF_SIZE) should have `ascending = false` for all elements. With `((i & stage) != 0)`, at stage=MERGE_BUF_SIZE, `i & stage` is always 0 (since i < stage), so ascending = false. So the final result is descending. Let me verify with a smaller example: For 4 elements [5, 2, 8, 1], MERGE_BUF_SIZE=4: Initial: [5, 2, 8, 1] Stage 2, step 1: - i=0: partner=1, i&2=0 → ascending=false. a=5 > b=2 → no swap. - i=2: partner=3, i&2=2 → ascending=true. a=8 > b=1 → swap (a > b). Result: [5, 8, 1, 2] Stage 4, step 1: - i=0: partner=1, i&4=0 → ascending=false. a=5 < b=8 → swap. Result: [8, 5, 1, 2] - i=2: partner=3, i&4=0 → ascending=false. a=1 < b=2 → swap. Result: [8, 5, 2, 1] Final: [8, 5, 2, 1] — descending! Correct! OK, the bitonic sort is correct. Let me check the block merge output: ```cuda if (tid < k) { out_values[row * k + tid] = block_val[tid]; out_indices[row * k + tid] = (long)block_idx[tid]; } ``` block_val is sorted descending, so block_val[0] is the largest element, block_val[k-1] is the k-th largest. We write elements 0..k-1 in order. This is correct. Hmm, so all the pieces seem correct. Let me check if there's an issue with the warp merge when partner data is read... Actually wait, I think I found the bug! In the warp merge: ```cuda merge_two(WBUF_V(src, lane), WBUF_I(src, lane), WBUF_V(src, partner), WBUF_I(src, partner), k, WBUF_V(dst, lane), WBUF_I(dst, lane)); ``` This MODIFIES `WBUF_V(src, lane)` in place. But then: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { WBUF_V(dst, lane)[i] = WBUF_V(src, lane)[i]; WBUF_I(dst, lane)[i] = WBUF_I(src, lane)[i]; } src = dst; dst = 1 - src; ``` In the NEXT round, `partner = lane ^ mask`. If the new partner's data was overwritten in the current round (because the partner was also processing and the partner's src buffer was modified), then the merge would use stale data. Wait, no. In round mask=1: - Lane 0 modifies src[0] and copies to dst[0]. Partner=1: reads src[1]. - Lane 1 modifies src[1] and copies to dst[1]. Partner=0: reads src[0]. Both lanes read from src buffer. The writes go to dst buffer. So lane 0 reads src[1] (OLD data, not modified by lane 1 since lane 1 writes to dst[1]). And lane 1 reads src[0] (OLD data). The src buffer is read-only in this round (writes go to dst). After the copy and swap, src = 1 (the old dst). Now src[0] has merged(0,1) and src[1] also has merged(0,1). In round mask=2: - Lane 0 reads src[0] (= merged(0,1)) and src[2] (= merged(2,3) from round 1). Merges. This seems correct. But wait: after round 1, src=1, dst=0. In round 2, we read from src=1 and write to dst=0. The merge function modifies src[0] in place. But src[0] (= old dst[0]) has merged(0,1). This gets overwritten with merged(0,1,2,3). Then we copy src[0] to dst[0]. But src[2] is also being modified by lane 2 in the same round! Lane 2 reads src[2] and src[0], merges, modifies src[2], copies to dst[2]. But lane 0 ALSO reads src[2] in its merge! So there's a race: lane 0 reads src[2] while lane 2 is potentially modifying src[2] (via merge_two). Wait, merge_two modifies its FIRST argument (src[2] for lane 2). But does it modify src[2] while lane 0 is reading it? Within a warp, shared memory writes are visible to other lanes immediately. So if lane 2 writes to src[2] before lane 0 reads it, lane 0 sees stale data. The fix: copy src→dst BEFORE the merge, and use dst as the read buffer for the merge. Or use a temp buffer. Actually, looking at my code more carefully: ```cuda merge_two(WBUF_V(src, lane), WBUF_I(src, lane), WBUF_V(src, partner), WBUF_I(src, partner), k, WBUF_V(dst, lane), WBUF_I(dst, lane)); ``` This modifies `WBUF_V(src, lane)` (the first argument). But it also READS `WBUF_V(src, partner)` (the second argument). The read happens during the merge, while the write to `WBUF_V(src, lane)` also happens during the merge. If lane A (with partner B) reads src[B] while lane B is also writing to src[B] (as part of lane B's own merge call), there IS a race. Specifically in round mask=1: - Lane 0: merge reads src[0] and src[1], writes to src[0] (modified) - Lane 1: merge reads src[1] and src[0], writes to src[1] (modified) Lane 0 reads src[1] while lane 1 writes to src[1]. RACE! But within a warp, all lanes execute in lockstep. The merge_two function has sequential code. So the read and write happen at different times: ```cuda while (out < k) { // Read from a_val[i] and b_val[j] (shared memory reads) // Write to sm_tmp_val[out] (shared memory write) } // Copy sm_tmp back to a_val (shared memory reads + writes) ``` During the while loop, lane 0 reads from src[1] (as b_val[j]). At the same time, lane 1 writes to sm_tmp_val[out] (lane 1's temp buffer, not src[0] or src[1]). So no race during the while loop! The race only happens during the copy-back phase: ```cuda for (int x = 0; x < k; x++) { a_val[x] = sm_tmp_val[x]; // write to src[lane] } ``` During this phase, lane 0 writes to src[0]. Lane 1 writes to src[1]. These are different locations, so no race! But wait, is there a race where one lane reads from src[partner] while the partner writes to src[partner] in the while loop? Let me trace: Lane 0's while loop iteration out=0: - Reads a_val[i] = src[0][i] (read src[0]) - Reads b_val[j] = src[1][j] (read src[1]) - Writes to sm_tmp_val[0] = dst[0][0] (write to dst buffer) Lane 1's while loop iteration out=0 (same iteration, lockstep): - Reads a_val[i] = src[1][i] (read src[1]) - Reads b_val[j] = src[0][j] (read src[0]) - Writes to sm_tmp_val[0] = dst[1][0] (write to dst buffer) No conflicts! The reads are from src buffer, writes to dst buffer. No write-read or write-write hazard! BUT, there IS a problem: both lanes read from src[0] and src[1]. Lane 0 writes to src[0] during the copy-back phase, while lane 1 might still be reading from src[0] in the while loop. Wait no, all lanes are in lockstep. The while loop executes synchronously across all lanes. The copy-back executes synchronously across all lanes. So: 1. All lanes execute the while loop (reading from src, writing to dst) 2. All lanes execute the copy-back (writing to src) No read-write hazard because the phases are separated. So actually, there's NO race condition! The lockstep execution within a warp ensures that all reads happen before all writes. OK so the warp merge should be correct. Let me think about what else could be wrong. import torch, reference, solution
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

# Simple test: small n, verify per-row top-k
for (batch, n, k) in [(1, 256, 4), (4, 512, 8), (1, 1024, 16)]:
    solution.batch = batch; solution.n = n; solution.k = k
    reference.batch = batch; reference.n = n; reference.k = k
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    x = reference.get_inputs()[0].to(device)
    
    sol_model = solution.Model(*solution.get_init_inputs()).to(device).eval()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*reference.get_init_inputs()).to(device).eval()
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        sv, si = sol_model(x)
        rv, ri = ref_model(x)
    
    # Compare values
    max_diff = (sv - rv).abs().max().item()
    # Compare gathered values
    gathered = torch.gather(x, dim=-1, index=si.long())
    gather_diff = (gathered - rv).abs().max().item()
    
    print(f'batch={batch} n={n} k={k}: max_val_diff={max_diff:.6f} gather_diff={gather_diff:.6f}')
    if max_diff > 1e-4 or gather_diff > 1e-4:
        print(f'  FAIL')
        print(f'  ref top-3 vals: {rv[0,:3]}')
        print(f'  sol top-3 vals: {sv[0,:3]}')
    else:
        print(f'  PASS')

The insertion into a sorted buffer is O(k), but it's done k times per round = O(k^2) per round. For k=64: 4096 insertions per round, each O(k) = 4096*64 = 262K ops per round. Way too slow. After warp 0 finishes its merge, warp 1 starts, etc. This requires inter-warp synchronization using barriers and a warp_counter: ```cuda __shared__ int warp_done; for (int w = 0; w < NUM_WARPS; w++) { if (warp_id == w) { // This warp does its merge using shared memory ... } __syncthreads(); } ``` After warp 0 finishes (and hits the barrier), warp 1 starts. For each batch, only B values need partner registers, reducing register pressure. ```cuda for (int mask = 1; mask < 32; mask <<= 1) { for (int batch = 0; batch < k; batch += BATCH_SIZE) { float p_val[BATCH_SIZE]; int p_idx[BATCH_SIZE]; for (int i = 0; i < BATCH_SIZE && batch+i < k; i++) { p_val[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[batch+i], mask); p_idx[i] = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[batch+i], mask); } // Now merge the batch... but I need the FULL partner list } } ``` But the problem: merge_two_sorted needs BOTH my_val[0..k-1] AND partner_val[0..k-1] simultaneously. If I only have BATCH_SIZE partner elements at a time, I can't do the full merge. **Alternative: merge one element at a time using insert.** Instead of using merge_two_sorted (which needs full lists), insert each partner element individually into my_val: ```cuda for (int mask = 1; mask < 32; mask <<= 1) { for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { float p_val = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[i], mask); int p_idx = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[i], mask); // Insert p_val into my sorted buffer if it belongs in top k if (p_val > my_val[k-1]) { // binary search + shift (same as per-thread scan) int lo = 0, hi = k-1; while (lo < hi) { ... } // shift and insert } } } ``` This is O(k^2) per merge round = O(64^2) = 4096 comparisons and shifts per round. With 5 rounds: 20480 ops per thread. At ~1 op/cycle: ~15 microseconds. Plus the per-thread scan (another ~5us). Total: ~20us per thread. Wait, this is PER WARP (32 threads execute concurrently). After processing all k=64 elements from the partner, lane i has the top k from both its original data and the partner's data. But wait, lane i shuffles ALL 64 values from lane i^mask. And lane i^mask shuffles ALL 64 values from lane i. Both lanes end up with the same top k. This is correct! The cost: k insertions per merge round, each O(k) insertion. 5 rounds * k * O(k) = 5 * 64 * 38 = 12160 ops. At 1.4 GHz: 8.7 microseconds. Plus per-thread scan (2-5us). Total: ~11-14 microseconds. Much better than 2.1ms! But there's a subtlety: the insertion uses binary search, which reads my_val values. Since my_val is being modified by the insertions, the binary search sees updated values. This is correct because we just want the top k from the combined set. Let me implement this approach: warp shuffle + per-element insertion. This eliminates ALL extra register arrays (no partner_val, no tmp_val). Only my_val and my_idx in registers. The trade-off: O(k^2) per merge round instead of O(k). But k <= 64, and O(64^2) = 4096 is still fast. Let me rewrite the kernel to use this approach.

59/64 wrong, max_abs_diff=0.87. The per-element insertion warp merge is not correctly producing the top-k. The issue: in the warp merge, when lane i inserts partner elements into its buffer, it processes partner elements in the order `my_val[0], my_val[1], ..., my_val[k-1]` from the partner. These elements are sorted (descending). Lane i inserts them one by one. But here's the subtlety: after inserting a partner element, `my_val` changes. When I then fetch the NEXT partner element via `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, my_val[i+1])`, the partner's `my_val` might also have changed (if the partner also received elements from lane i). Wait, no. Within a warp mask round: - Lane i shuffles my_val[j] from lane i^mask for j=0..k-1 - Lane i^mask shuffles my_val[j] from lane i for j=0..k-1 - BOTH lanes shuffle ALL values BEFORE either lane starts modifying its buffer Actually, that's not true. Looking at the code: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { float p_val = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[i], mask); int p_idx = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[i], mask); // insert p_val into my_val } ``` Lane A shuffles from lane B's my_val[0], then inserts. Lane B shuffles from lane A's my_val[0], then inserts. The shuffle reads the VALUE of my_val[i] from the partner's register. Since all lanes execute in lockstep, lane A reads lane B's my_val[0] BEFORE lane B modifies my_val (the insertion hasn't happened yet for either lane). But in the insertion step: ```cuda for (int j = k - 1; j > pos; j--) { my_val[j] = my_val[j - 1]; my_idx[j] = my_idx[j - 1]; } my_val[pos] = p_val; my_idx[pos] = p_idx; ``` This modifies my_val[0..k-1]. After this modification, the NEXT shuffle (i=1) reads `my_val[1]` from the partner. But the partner's my_val[1] might have been modified by the partner's insertion! Actually, this is correct! Both lanes shuffle the ORIGINAL my_val values (before any modification) for all i=0..k-1. BUT the insertion modifies my_val in place. So when we go to i=1, my_val (local) has been modified. But we're SHUFFLING from the partner, whose my_val has also been modified. The issue: when lane A shuffles lane B's my_val[1], lane B has already processed its own insertion for i=0. So lane B's my_val has changed. Lane A sees lane B's MODIFIED my_val[1], which might not be the original value. But is this correct? Lane A wants to merge with lane B. If lane B has already processed some of lane A's elements, then lane B's buffer has some merged results. Lane A shuffles these merged results (which include some of lane A's own elements). If lane A then inserts these merged results into its own buffer, it's essentially doing a redundant merge. Wait, this is actually the standard butterfly merge pattern! In the butterfly merge: - Round 1: pairs of threads exchange data and merge - Round 2: pairs of merged pairs exchange and merge - ... Each thread's buffer accumulates results from more threads at each round. For the per-element insertion approach: - Round mask=1: lane i inserts all k elements from lane i^1. After this, lane i has the top k from threads i and i^1. - Round mask=2: lane i inserts all k elements from lane i^2 (which already has the merged result from threads i^2 and i^3). After this, lane i has the top k from threads i, i^1, i^2, i^3. - ... This is CORRECT for the butterfly merge pattern! But there's a subtlety: when lane i inserts partner element `my_val[j]` from lane i^mask, the partner's `my_val[j]` is the j-th largest element in the partner's buffer (since my_val is sorted descending). Lane i inserts this element into its own buffer. After k insertions, lane i should have the top k from its original data and the partner's data. But wait — the insertion order matters! Lane i inserts partner's my_val[0] (largest), then my_val[1] (second largest), ..., then my_val[k-1] (smallest). After inserting the largest first, my_val changes. If the partner's my_val[1] (second largest) is smaller than lane i's my_val[k-1] after the first insertion, it won't be inserted. This is correct — it means the partner's second largest is not in lane i's top k. But what if the partner's my_val[1] IS in lane i's top k? Then it gets inserted. But by the time we process my_val[2], my_val[3], etc., lane i's buffer may have filled up with larger elements from lane i's original data, and the partner's smaller elements might not fit. This is all correct behavior for a merge! So why is the output wrong? Let me think... Actually, I think the issue might be that we're shuffling from MODIFIED partner data, not the original partner data. Let me trace: For mask=1, partner = lane ^ 1: - Lane 0: shuffles from lane 1's my_val[0..k-1]. Inserts. - Lane 1: shuffles from lane 0's my_val[0..k-1]. Inserts. At i=0: lane 0 reads lane 1's my_val[0] (original). Lane 1 reads lane 0's my_val[0] (original). Both insert. At i=1: lane 0 reads lane 1's my_val[1]. But lane 1 has JUST modified its buffer (inserted lane 0's my_val[0]). So lane 1's my_val[1] has changed! Lane 0 reads the modified value. But this is WRONG! The butterfly merge expects to exchange both full buffers, then merge. With the per-element approach, we're exchanging and merging ONE ELEMENT AT A TIME, which means the partner's buffer changes between shuffles. For the butterfly merge to work correctly with per-element insertion, the insertion by lane 1 should NOT affect what lane 0 reads. But since they're in the same warp and my_val is in registers, the shuffle reads the CURRENT register value, which has been modified. FIX: I need to shuffle ALL k values BEFORE doing any insertion. reasoning (2845 chars)
Let me wait for the output while also thinking about potential issues with this design. Actually, there's still a potential issue with the write-back in the butterfly merge: ```cuda // Write merged result back to shared memory for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { WSM_V(lane)[i] = my_val[i]; WSM_I(lane)[i] = my_idx[i]; } ``` This writes the merged result to the lane's own slot in shared memory. But at the same time, other lanes in the warp might also be writing to their own slots. Since all lanes write to different slots (lane determines the slot), there's no conflict. But there's a subtle issue: after the write-back, the next mask round reads from the NEW data in shared memory. The butterfly merge pattern is: - Round mask=1: lanes (0,1), (2,3), ... exchange and merge. After write-back, lane 0 has merged(0,1), lane 1 has merged(0,1), etc. - Round mask=2: lanes (0,2), (1,3), ... exchange and merge. Lane 0 reads lane 2's data (= merged(2,3)), merges with its own (= merged(0,1)). Result: merged(0,1,2,3). This is correct! After each round, the shared memory is updated with the merged results. But wait: the write-back happens in the `for (int mask ...)` loop. After mask=1 write-back, the shared memory has the merged results for all lanes. Then mask=2 reads from shared memory — the data includes merged results from mask=1. This is the intended butterfly merge behavior. In the original v1 approach (register-based __shfl_xor_sync), the merge was: ```cuda for (int mask = 1; mask < 32; mask <<= 1) { // shuffle all k elements from partner into partner_val // merge_two_sorted(my_val, my_idx, partner_val, partner_idx, k) // Result: my_val has merged top-k } ``` The register-based approach automatically "writes back" because my_val is modified in place and the next shuffle reads the updated my_val from the partner. In the shared memory approach, after the insertions modify my_val (registers), I explicitly write my_val back to shared memory. Then the next mask round reads from shared memory. This achieves the same effect. The only potential issue: within a single mask round, lane A reads lane B's data from shared memory, and lane B reads lane A's data. Both read the SAME (pre-merge) data because neither has written back yet. The write-back happens AFTER both lanes have finished reading and inserting. So the reads are correct. But there's a lockstep issue: all lanes in the warp execute in lockstep. The read loop (reading partner data) executes for all lanes before the write-back loop. So: 1. All lanes read partner data (from pre-merge shared memory) 2. All lanes insert partner elements into their register buffers 3. All lanes write back to shared memory This is correct because the reads all happen before any writes. OK, the design should be correct. Let me wait for the check.py output.

reasoning (5147 chars)
Still terrible performance. Shape 4 (k=1) is OK at 0.066ms vs eager 0.046ms. But the other shapes are very slow: - Shape 0 (k=64): 3.96ms vs 0.071ms ref — 56x slower! - Shape 1 (k=8): 0.25ms vs 0.075ms — 3.3x slower - Shape 2 (k=32): 1.42ms vs 0.075ms — 19x slower - Shape 3 (k=16): 0.59ms vs 0.074ms — 8x slower The problem is clear: the O(k^2) per-element insertion in the warp merge is a bottleneck for large k. For k=64: 5 mask rounds * 64 elements * O(64) insertion = 5 * 64 * 32 = ~10,240 shifts per warp merge. With 8 warps: 81,920 shifts. Plus per-thread scan: 512 elements * ~20 insertions * 32 shifts = 327,680 shifts. Total: ~410K shifts. At 1.4 GHz, that's about 290 microseconds of shift operations, plus overhead. But the measured time is 3.96ms, which is 14x more than 290 microseconds. So there's still register spilling or other overhead. Actually, wait. The per-element insertion in the warp merge is: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { float p_val = WSM_V(partner)[i]; // shared memory read int p_idx = WSM_I(partner)[i]; // shared memory read if (p_val <= my_val[k - 1]) continue; // register read + compare // binary search: ~6 iterations, reading my_val (registers) // shift: up to 63 elements in registers // insert } ``` The shared memory reads from `WSM_V(partner)` are uncoalesced (since partner = lane ^ mask, and lane varies). With 32 lanes reading from 32 different locations, the shared memory bank conflicts could be significant. But the main bottleneck is still likely the O(k) shift within the insertion. For k=64, each shift copies up to 64 float pairs = 128 register copies. With 64 insertions per mask round and 5 rounds: 64 * 5 * 32 (avg) * 2 (val+idx) = 20,480 register copies per thread. Plus per-thread scan: ~100 insertions * 32 * 2 = 6,400 copies. Total: ~27,000 register copies. At 1 cycle per register copy (best case with register-to-register moves): 27,000 cycles = 19 microseconds. But with register spilling, each "register" copy might be a local memory access (30+ cycles). This would explain the 3.96ms. So register spilling is STILL an issue! The my_val[MAX_K] and my_idx[MAX_K] arrays are 128 registers, which leaves only 127 registers for everything else. The compiler might still spill some of these to local memory. The root cause: MAX_K=64 is too large for register-only storage. I need to reduce the per-thread buffer size. **Solution: Reduce per-thread k** Instead of each thread keeping k=64 elements, split the k elements across multiple threads within a warp. For example: - Each thread keeps k/4 = 16 elements (32 registers for val + idx) - 4 threads cooperate to maintain the top-k for their "group" - After the scan, the group merges their top-16 into the group's top-64 But this complicates the warp merge significantly. **Alternative: Use a different warp merge approach** Instead of per-element insertion in registers, use shared memory for the entire warp merge. The warp merge copies data to shared memory, sorts in shared memory (bitonic sort of 32*64=2048 elements), and extracts top k. 2048 elements in shared memory = 2048 * 8 = 16KB. Bitonic sort of 2048 elements: log2(2048) = 11 stages, stages = 11*12/2 = 66 barrier steps. With 32 threads per warp: each thread handles 64 elements per step. 66 barriers within a single warp! But wait — __syncthreads() within a warp is free (all lanes are already synchronized). So the bitonic sort in shared memory within a single warp has NO barrier overhead! For the bitonic sort of 2048 elements with 32 threads: - Each thread handles 2048/32 = 64 elements per comparison step - Each of the 66 steps does 64 comparisons + 64 conditional swaps - Total: 66 * 64 = 4224 ops per thread - At 1.4 GHz: ~3 microseconds Plus loading data (64 ops) and extracting result (64 ops). Total: ~3.5 microseconds per warp. With 8 sequential warps: 8 * 3.5us = 28us. Much better than 3.96ms! But wait, the bitonic sort needs the data in shared memory. And each warp uses the same shared memory sequentially. The data needs to be loaded by the warp's threads. For a sort of 2048 elements, with 32 threads: each thread loads 64 elements from registers to shared memory. Then 66 bitonic stages. Then each thread extracts 2 elements (64/32 = 2). Actually, I want the full bitonic sort to find the top k. After sorting all 2048 elements, the top k are at positions 0..k-1 (descending order). Threads 0..k-1 read their result. So the warp merge becomes: 1. Copy my_val → shared memory (all 32 lanes, 64 elements each) 2. Bitonic sort 2048 elements in shared memory (66 stages, no barriers needed within warp) 3. Extract top k from shared memory This is much cleaner and should be much faster. Let me implement this. The total shared memory needed: - warp_sm_val: 32 * 64 * 4 = 8KB (OR 2048 * 4 = 8KB — same thing) - warp_sm_idx: 32 * 64 * 4 = 8KB - block_val: 512 * 4 = 2KB - block_idx: 512 * 4 = 2KB Total: 20KB. Fits in 48KB! And the sequential warp execution means only one warp uses warp_sm at a time. The 8 __syncthreads() barriers between warps add ~1us overhead. Let me implement this!

reasoning (4900 chars)
Wait, there's a fundamental correctness issue with this design. Each thread only maintains BUF_SIZE=16 elements. For k=64, this means each thread only keeps its top 16 elements, not the top 64. Then the warp merge combines 32 threads × 16 elements = 512 elements and extracts the top k=64 via bitonic sort. Is this correct? YES! Each thread independently selects the top 16 from its chunk. The warp merge sorts all 512 (= 32×16) elements and extracts the top 64. Since the true top-64 must be among the threads' per-thread top-16, this is correct: - The true 64th largest element overall must be among the threads' 16th largest elements (since if a thread has more than 16 of the top 64, it would need to have at least 17 of the top 64, but each thread only processes 1/256 of the data, so at most k/256 = 64/256 = 0.25 of the top 64 would fall in one thread's chunk — actually that's not quite right). Hmm wait, this is NOT guaranteed to be correct! If the top 64 elements all happen to fall in the same thread's chunk (thread 0's positions 0, 256, 512, ...), then thread 0's top 16 would include 16 of the top 64, but 64-16=48 of the top 64 would be LOST because each thread only keeps 16 elements! This is a real issue for striped partitioning. With block partitioning (each thread gets a contiguous chunk), the issue is less severe but still possible. For example, if n=1024, k=64, and BUF_SIZE=16: each thread (256 threads) processes 4 elements. Thread 0's 4 elements might ALL be in the top 64. But threads 1-255's elements might also include top-64 elements. With BUF_SIZE=16, each thread can keep all 4 elements (BUF_SIZE > 4), so no issue here. But if n is large (e.g., 131072) and BUF_SIZE=16: each thread processes 512 elements. The top 64 elements might be distributed across threads. Could one thread have more than 16 of the top 64? With striped partitioning (stride=256), each thread's elements are: i, i+256, i+512, ..., i+511*256. If the input values are random (randn), the probability that thread i has more than 16 of the top 64 is very small. But it's not zero. For random data, the expected count per thread is 64/256 = 0.25, so having 16+ is astronomically unlikely. But the check script uses random data (randn). With truly random values, the probability of one thread having more than 16 of the top 64 is negligible. So in practice, this should work. However, with sorted or adversarially constructed data, it could fail. For the benchmark, the data is random (randn), so it should be fine. But wait, the check uses `torch.randn` with seed 42. The values are random Gaussian. The top 64 elements might be somewhat clustered but unlikely to have 16+ in one thread's chunk. Let me be more careful: what BUF_SIZE do I need to guarantee correctness? Worst case: all top-k elements are in one thread's chunk. With striped partitioning (stride=BLOCK_SIZE=256), the top-k elements could be at positions i, i+256, i+512, ... (all same thread i). For k=64: positions i, i+256, ..., i+63*256. All these positions are in thread i's chunk. So thread i's chunk contains ALL top-64 elements. For correctness, BUF_SIZE must be ≥ k. Otherwise, some top-k elements would be lost. With BUF_SIZE=16 and k=64, the design is INCORRECT in the worst case! Fix: I need BUF_SIZE ≥ k. But BUF_SIZE=64 would mean 128 registers for my_val + my_idx, plus other temps. This brings back the register spilling issue. Alternative: use multi-pass approach where the warp merge sorts all elements from all threads. But this requires loading ALL elements into shared memory. For k=64: each thread has processed 512 elements. The top-64 might be distributed. If I load ALL 512 elements per thread into shared memory: 256 * 512 = 131,072 elements = 1 MB. Won't fit. OK, I need BUF_SIZE=64. Let me go back to the approach where each thread keeps k elements, but avoid register spilling in the warp merge by using shared memory for the warp-level bitonic sort. The warp merge: 32 threads × 64 elements = 2048 elements. Sort in shared memory. Shared memory needed: 2048 * 8 = 16 KB. Fits! The per-thread scan still has my_val[64] in registers (128 regs). The warp merge copies my_val to shared memory, then does a bitonic sort in shared memory. No extra register arrays needed! This is actually the simplest and most correct design: 1. Per-thread scan with k elements in registers (insertion sort) 2. Copy to shared memory 3. Warp-level bitonic sort (in shared memory, 32*k elements) 4. Extract top k 5. Block merge: bitonic sort of 8 warp results The register usage: my_val[64] (64 regs) + my_idx[64] (64 regs) = 128 regs. Plus temps: ~20 regs. Total: ~148 regs. Fits in 255! The compiler might still spill some of my_val or my_idx to local memory if it runs out of registers for other purposes. But 148 regs out of 255 should be safe. Let me set BUF_SIZE back to 64 (or dynamic based on k).

+It is competitive at very small k but the O(k²) shuffle volume and insertion
+overhead for k=64 make it slower than torch.topk's CUB-backed radix-select

+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Merge two sorted-descending lists (a, b) of length k into a.
+// Uses per-warp shared-memory temp storage to avoid register spill.
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+__device__ __forceinline__ void merge_two(
+    float* __restrict__ a_val, int* __restrict__ a_idx,
+    const float* __restrict__ b_val, const int* __restrict__ b_idx,
+    int k,
+    float* sm_tmp_val, int* sm_tmp_idx)
+{
+    int i = 0, j = 0, out = 0;
+    #pragma unroll 1
+    while (out < k) {
+        bool take_a;
+        if (i >= k)      take_a = false;
+        else if (j >= k) take_a = true;
+        else             take_a = (a_val[i] >= b_val[j]);
+        if (take_a) { sm_tmp_val[out] = a_val[i]; sm_tmp_idx[out] = a_idx[i]; i++; }
+        else        { sm_tmp_val[out] = b_val[j]; sm_tmp_idx[out] = b_idx[j]; j++; }
+        out++;
+    }
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int x = 0; x < MAX_K; x++) {
+        if (x < k) { a_val[x] = sm_tmp_val[x]; a_idx[x] = sm_tmp_idx[x]; }
+    }
+}

Instead of shuffling partner data element-by-element, do the warp merge entirely using shared memory, but with ALL warps executing concurrently. Each warp gets its own region of shared memory. With 8 warps and 2048 elements per warp: 8 * 2048 * 8 bytes = 128 KB. Won't fit. But what if each warp only needs a smaller buffer? For the merge, each warp needs to store the current state of all 32 lanes. That's 32 * k elements * 8 bytes = 32 * 64 * 8 = 16 KB per warp. With 8 warps: 128 KB. Still won't fit. What if I use a different warp merge that doesn't need per-warp buffers? The warp merge using __shfl_xor_sync with register arrays works — it's just slow due to spilling. The per-element approach with __shfl_xor_sync is correct in theory but has the partner-data-modification issue. Hmm, wait. Let me re-examine the per-element approach. The issue was: 1. Lane i shuffles partner's my_val[j] 2. Lane i inserts the shuffled value into its own buffer 3. Lane i's buffer changes, affecting what the partner sees when it shuffles from lane i But in the butterfly merge pattern, after lane i modifies its buffer by inserting partner's element, the partner would try to shuffle lane i's my_val[j+1] in the next iteration. But lane i's my_val[j+1] might have been shifted by the insertion. Actually, wait. In the per-element approach: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { float p_val = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_val[i], mask); int p_idx = __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, my_idx[i], mask); // Insert p_val into my_val (modifies my_val in place) } ``` Lane A processes i=0: shuffles lane B's my_val[0]. Inserts. Lane A's my_val changes. Lane B processes i=0: shuffles lane A's my_val[0]. Lane A's my_val[0] has NOT changed yet (the insert hasn't happened since all lanes execute the shuffle first). Wait, actually the shuffle and insert happen in SEQUENCE within the loop body: 1. Shuffle (reads partner's register) 2. Insert (modifies my register) Within a warp, all lanes execute the LOOP in lockstep. So: Iteration i=0: All lanes: shuffle partner's my_val[0] → p_val All lanes: insert p_val into my_val Iteration i=1: All lanes: shuffle partner's my_val[1] → p_val ← BUT partner's my_val[1] might have been SHIFTED by the insert! All lanes: insert p_val into my_val So in iteration i=1, lane A shuffles lane B's my_val[1]. But lane B's my_val[1] was potentially modified by lane B's own insertion during iteration i=0. This is the issue. The partner's data changes in-place between shuffle iterations. FIX: shuffle ALL values BEFORE doing any insertions. But that requires storing k shuffled values. Or: use an approach where the shuffled values are independent of the insertions. One way: use a temporary copy of the partner's data in shared memory. Here's the approach that should work: 1. Each warp writes its my_val/my_idx to a per-warp shared memory region (16 KB per warp) 2. Each lane then reads partner data from the SHARED MEMORY copy (which doesn't change during the insertions) 3. After the insertions, write the updated my_val back to shared memory for the next mask round This requires per-warp shared memory, which we established doesn't fit for 8 concurrent warps. BUT we can use sequential warp execution (8 barriers), which adds ~1us overhead but is correct. Given my time constraints, let me go with the sequential warp approach using shared memory (v3/v5 approach, which passed correctness) and accept the performance. The peak_fraction will be LOW but the kernel is correct. Let me restore the v5 approach (which passed check.py).

Let me try one more quick optimization: the per-element insertion in the warp merge is O(k²). What if I make it O(k log k) by sorting in shared memory instead? For each warp (sequential), I can: 1. Copy all 32*k elements to shared memory 2. Sort them (bitonic sort in shared memory, 66 steps within a single warp, no barriers) 3. Extract top k This is exactly what v6 did, but v6 was very slow (6.45ms for shape 0). Let me check why... Actually, v6 had a different issue: WARP_MERGE_SIZE=2048 meant a full 2048-element bitonic sort even when k was small. For k=8: 32*8=256 elements are valid, the rest are NEG_INF. The sort still processes all 2048 elements. But 2048-element sort with 32 threads takes 66 steps × 64 elements/thread = 4224 shared memory accesses per thread per warp. With 8 warps: 33792 accesses. The per-element insertion approach (current v5) also takes 5 mask rounds × 64 elements × O(64) = 20480 ops per thread. But each "op" is a register-based binary search + shift. The shift is O(k) register copies. The shared memory bitonic sort should be FASTER because shared memory accesses are cheaper than register-to-register copies (with spilling). But the results show otherwise (6.45ms vs 6.33ms — similar). The fundamental issue is that for k=64, BOTH approaches are dominated by the O(k) per-element work. The only way to beat torch.topk is to use a fundamentally different algorithm (radix select) which is O(n) with low constant factors.
